RATING: 3
The Good: It's a really thinky game; getting the mancala to do what you want, budgeting appropriate time levels, figuring out how to milk each area to your advantage. The Bad: It's a bunch of mini-games bolted onto the core mancala action selection mechanism. Not a lot of theme connections in that regard. The Ugly: There is almost zero player interaction, if someone runs away with the game, you have to hope they screw up because coming back is woefully hard once that player figures out how the milk the mancala for what it's worth. Plays best at 4p as time management becomes more critical and the rest of the mini-games become more contested. I found I liked the game until I figured out what made it tick, then the shine wore off.

The mancala mechanism is brilliant and really makes me think long and hard about every decision. It really forces you to think multiple turns ahead so you don't screw yourself. Every round and every game of this will have you deciding which part of the board you should focus on. They all generate points, but to find which combination at what time is the crux of the game.

RATING: 9
Amazing, amazing, amazing. Almost sandboxy in the freedom of routes you can take but there's a gently mounting tension throughout due to the jostling for the particular spots / tiles you want, the fickle demands of the people and the constant march of time. You'll have one or two primary focus points yet you'll want to respond to the changing board position. And all this has to be filtered through the delicious Mancala action selection. Fast became my favourite Feld - not as breezy as Castles of Burgundy, but ultimately more satisfying.

RATING: 8
A victory points (VP) grabfest with a thin but pleasant enough Roman theme.

The game consists of 4 years, with each year consisting of 4 rounds, with a nice twist in that the length of a round is variable depending on the actions that players choose.  The core mechanic is unique (at least to me) in that you move clockwise and stepwise, action markers around a "rondel" of sorts and choose the action where you placed last with your action marker.  There are 6 different actions to choose from, and all of them are generally good although some are significantly better than others depending on the situation/your setup/end of game bonus cards etc.

After movement of action markers, you can potentially complete a bonus trajan tile (which awards points and a one-time ability bonus) if you've set yourself up/planned appropriately, then you get the one action you landed on.  Actions are: 1)military conquests, which have multiple options, but which can score points, 2) construction, which scores one-time points and either allows for big end game points or one-time immediate second action, 3) trajan tile selection, which can be used to replenish your "rondel" and thus score you more points and get you more benefits, 4) shipping, which is an opportunity to score big points by playing goods cards in one of 3 different possible combinations, 5) senate action, which scores points, and positions you for first choice at end of round bonus tiles, which can score big points at game end, and 6) forum, which is where you grab tiles such as votes for senate, requirements for the people, extra action bonuses.

At the end of a year, you need to satisfy the needs of the people or lose negative points (kind of like feeding your family in Agricola but not as punitive, and not as punitive as some other Feld games).  Again at end game, points are scored in addition to the points accumulated in game (the in-game points are very significant as well as the end-game ones).  Seems to scale very well (2-4 players), solid components, smooth and easy to play, but alot of options and difficult to determine which is the best one.  Definitely a solid euro gamers game.

RATING: 2
[I]tl;dr - Have fun playing 12 mini games and adding up your points at the end.[/I]
[COLOR=#CCCCFF]____________________________[/COLOR]

Trajan is a brain-burning Feld game that's more boring than being at work. If you want a game where you're struggling against the design for the entire game, and all of the disparate elements feel totally disconnected, this is the game for you. Though I pursued a strong military strategy, I was destroyed in the Forum and finished almost forty points behind my father-in-law.

Ultimately, I felt like I was playing 12 different mini-games rather than a single cohesive whole. If you want to keep balanced in your head the relative strengths of each little mini-game and relatively how much of each you should pursue based on what your opponents are doing, Trajan may be the game for you. 

One piece of praise: the rondel element was extremely thought-provoking, but one of the best parts of the game -- I'd love to see that rondel mechanic spun off into its own game. I'm not sure what that would look like, but the actual action-selection in Trajan was quite interesting. 

Recommended if point salad is your thing, I guess. Can I damn with faint praise? I don't think Trajan is a good game.

RATING: 10
Potential future 10 if I like it on replays. Am I good at this game? No, not yet. However, it's by far my favorite Feld (comparing to Bora Bora & Amerigo). For all the complexity of this game having so many actions, all the actions have logical, easy-to-remember rules. It's definitely a heavy, complex game that rewards a mix of strategy & tactics. You have to consider long term strategies but also seize opportunities. In my opinion, this is the most thematic Feld I've played. You are a top-ranking politician in an empire and there's always so much you want to do and always either no time to do it (those round tracks flow by so quickly!) or no opportunity to do it (if you haven't counted out the mancala properly by planning in advance). There are so many objectives and completing one often means sacrificing the other. Do you meet the people's demands? Well, that'll cost a turn, and you could use that turn to ship goods. Or you could use that turn to place your legions and gain power over an area. Or you might build, and if you don't build now, another politician may occupy that area before you get to it. But wait - who is trying to win leadership in the senate? Oh if only you could get that forum tile to buy some votes, you could get that valuable end-game scoring tile... and so on. Trajan is a game of scarcity. You want to do a great deal, you have to do multiple things to survive, but you also can't run around doing everything and accomplishing nothing. You would think it would cause AP, but due to the limiting nature of the mancala, decisions are actually quite easy to make. Normally, by the time your turn comes around, you're ready to go. For a game that takes 2+ hours for 4 players, it actually moves quite swiftly. It is a hard game to teach because of the large amount of actions and the mancala mechanic (I'd recommend Five Tribes as a simpler precursor to teach players that mechanic first),  but it is definitely worth the effort. A fair warning that the rulebook is painfully dry and exhausting to read, but once you get past that, there is an excellent game here.

6/7/2017 - played again, updating to a 10. Originally, we made several rule mistakes and getting those rules right has made the game even better (the three rules was taking a bonus action when taking the first construction tile of a type, that trajan tiles go under the arch, and that playing to the display allows you to redraw cards). The systems are designed to work together in perfect, brutal harmony. A masterpiece of a game that is rich in depth and yet by some miracle completely enjoyable by absolutely new players (because everything is designed to avoid causing AP). Given how much better the game is with those rules played right (especially the construction action allowing for mandala mitigation), I just have to raise my rating to 10. Absolutely love this game.

6/21/17 - Played again. Yes, definitely a 10.

RATING: 7
I found this another bulky design by Feld. This is set-collection in a variety of different forms with no thematic integration. It looks lovely, works well, and is nicely strategic, but sometimes less is more. This game is not as unwieldy as Bora Bora, but it is not as streamlined as Notre Dame, In the Year of the Dragon, Castles of Burgundy, or Rialto. The Mancala mechanic is fabulous - why Feld feels the need to throw so many eggs into one basket is beyond me though - a simpler use of the Mancala/Rondel system would have kept me much happier. Luna is still number-one Feld for me, which is kind of a shame because it's the least obviously "Feldian". Still, if you enjoy his other games, Trajan is a sure-fire hit.

RATING: 7
1 play / 3 players

LIKE: Great mancala use, managing the limited colors and thinking ahead to the next action or two, AND managing bonus actions. I really enjoyed how the different strategies evolved over the course of the game, and how players have opportunities to interfere with one another.

DISLIKE: The different areas on the board are a little disparate and don't merge together intuitively. In my opinion, this accentuates the point salad element in a negative way. Also, the shipping cards are very random and it doesn't seem that the game moves through the deck quickly enough to mitigate its randomness.

OVERALL: A very good game, lots to enjoy. Some mild similarities to Oracle of Delphi. The manacala is a brain burner, delightfully so, but not sure how often I would play this.

RATING: 6.5
Trajan isn't a great game. At least, that's my first impression. I do like it well enough - and I want to play it more. But my take away is the Mancala mechanism is surprisingly complicated for little result. It turns out there's only one purpose for the colors and that's to get the bonus Trajan tiles (appropriately the name of the game). Yes, those are great and they add a different angle on the game. But it seems overly complex to have that result of what is nearly just a free turn. So there's a frustration to this central mechanic that overshadows the fun for me. Then around this mechanic are what many now call victory point salad. That's alright for me - it keeps you thinking and always puts out a reward. But over all, Trajan just isn't blowing me away. I am glad that is plays at all given that I'm color-blind!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
More is not always better, I could say. In the heart of Trajan is an action selection rondel mechanic, which is a neat solution for everyone who like to plan ahead and phase their actions. So this should be a game for me since I like phasing. 

However, in Trajan things have been taken a step or two too far. You're not only setting up for your next couple of actions but almost the whole game. You are not creating combos but piping your actions into certain order. I reckon, this is mainly because the situation on the board via player interaction changes too slowly to keep the planning horizon from stretching 6-7 rounds to the future. Personally, I think this is just too much. Oh, I do adore games that leave room for long term strategies but it is not the same thing as planning all your moves beforehand.

This pre-planning creates a problem: You definitely do not want to mess your carefully planned sequence of actions but sometimes the tactical situation might require something else. Often a single miscalculated step puts you off the track and you will spend several turns to get back on it. In a brutal game like Trajan, this usually equals to giving up the fight for the win. So what happens is that everyone will just concentrate on their own rondel and player interaction will become even less. Solitairish games are usually not a problem but Trajan is all about being first in whatever you do.

There are a lot of good qualities to this game and I can see why it is highly rated. It just did not hit the sweetspot for me who likes pondering the subtler consequences of my actions instead of leaping from one cliff to another with no margin for error. But at least you have meaningful decisions to make throughout the whole game.

One more good thing is how you get points from almost everything you do. Some people criticize Feld games for this, i.e., being VP salads but I quite fancy it. Those single VPs you get from myriad of things do not win you the game. They are just consolidation prizes giving you a feeling that you achieved something. You do not need to perform a ten move sequence to score points but managing nine moves is already rewarding.

RATING: 8.95
Abstract:
A heavy euro optimization game by Stefan Feld where each player has his own game board with a Mancala (rondel wheel) to activate one of six sub-systems and potential bonus actions (Trajan tiles).

Possible actions:
a) Forum: select a forum tile (get an update tile to increase the number of actions)
b) Senate: gain senate vote (get victory points, choose end game bonus tile on majority)
c) Construction: construct buildings (set-collection + cut-off other players "snake"-like)
d) Military: conquer a province (area control, collect bonus tiles)
e) Shipping: gain good card or ship resources (set-collection, race-game)
f) Trajan: gain a Trajan tile (placed on the Mancala to trigger additional actions)

Pros:
+ Innovative use of Mancala which is all about efficiency and planning 
+ Player interaction is indirect and depends on the particular sub-game: area-control, race for collecting tiles first, snatching away good tiles to avoid penalties caused by demands
+ Wide variety of options that feel different (high replayability)
+ Rules are good (could be more explicit: Trajan tiles satisfy demands only after being activated, player order never changes, and time tracker is not reset after a round)
+ Length of a round is determined how players use their Mancala
+ Good mix between tactical and strategical decision-making, however, strategical players will be higher rewarded
+ Plays well with all player counts (2-player is less competitive, because their is more space/tiles available)

Neutrals:
# Nearly no luck: demand tiles are randomly revealed and players can choose different ways how to draw new good cards
# Good cards and forum tiles are revealed in different order but there are no different elements or abilities per game
# No hidden information
# Scoring happens during the round, between rounds, and at the end of the game

Cons:
- Overwhelming on first play: many options and mechanics is not for everyone
- Fiddly: lots of tiles
- Long set-up due to lot of tiles (Trajan tiles need to be sorted)
- Can be solitaire - if you consider what others do, it will get complex (analysis paralysis)
- Theme is okay, but dry and not very strong
- Hard to teach: a lot of different systems
- No player aid

Thoughts:
Trajan is about planing and puzzling to be most efficient and sometimes being ahead of someone else. I like the intertwined sub-games most. The Mancala drives the game and makes it unique. So far, the Mancala intimidates me into planing at least five or more turns ahead.

Similar games: 
* Bora Bora (complexity-wise, similar number of mechanics and tiles)

Player: 2-4 (best: 3-4) | Time: 60-120m

RATING: 5.5
A never ending game of mankala with columns of differing colors. Worse part of it is playing it solo. No one really cared what each other was doing. Maybe some part of the game we would be concentrating on 'getting there first.'

But as it dragged on, no one bothers, as even if you did, you will just be slowing down the leader to let the others catch up. In which, you will go further down.

Move to A to get B for VP type of game again. Worst, is that never ending cycle to want to go A,but you need to go 2 rounds before you come back to A. Thus the game drops down to moving only 1-2 peg, just to repeat what we need to do. A more frustrated version of worker placement game, except, that you can't really place it. Rodel in a different format. Afraid nothing really genius about it.

RATING: 7
Dry as saltines, like most Field games. Definitely better than some of his others for me. Planning for combos on the mancala player board is interesting enough. But I'm trying to figure out if there are enough competitive routes to victory. 

My experience was grossly weighted towards senate / shipping combos and collecting sets of the builder (right term?) tokens. Can military or trade heavy strategies compete? Or is that it?

Like any Feld game, the actions themselves have little depth. It's take a step, claim a tile, or move a token on a track. Woohoo. If the game was abstracted to 4 tracks with status markers moving up W/the mancala combos and cards on top, it would be virtually the exact same game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I am beginning to see a pattern here with Feld games:  lots of quirky and interesting mechanics very capably put together, with lots of different paths to victory to explore on multiple plays, and...virtually no real theme, or sense of thematic play, very little direct player interaction, and very rudimentary, ho-hum spatial elements and challenges.  

Trajan may very well be the pinnacle of this Feld style, but for me, curiously, after really admiring Macao and Castles for this Feld style, with Trajan, it feels like it is beginning to wear thin and appear inadequate from a game design perspective.  Where is the player interaction in this game?  Where is an actual theme?  Is it asking too much to play a game where you feel like you are thematically doing something other than trying to cleverly produce victory points using clever mechanics?  (For what it's worth, I think Feld's Luna fares better on theme, player interaction, and interesting spatial aspects.) 

And as much as I admire Feld's use of a circular mancala mechanic here in Trajan, for me, it really continually underscored that this is a game, devoid of theme, where 75% of your playing time is spent trying to manage your game of solitaire, going around in circles all game.  In truth, I guess I don't care for games where the dynamo, the driver of the game, is a process of going around in circles (eg rondelles).  Why am I staring at a small "solitaire defined" circle for 2 hours?  

So Trajan is definitely a challenging and interesting game.  Fun for dedicated gamers for sure.  But ultimately, Trajan was so thin thematically, it felt to me like much ado about nothing, absolutely nothing.  Giving it a 7.  April 2012.

No desire to play this again, so giving it a 6.  October 2014.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.9
I tend to think of "point salad" as a disparaging term, and Trajan is as point-salady as they come. It's essentially a set of 6 areas (mini-games) tied together by a mancala that controls which of the areas you can take actions in. But Feld knows how to make a truly compelling game out of it. Key to what makes the game interesting is the variable set-up and the variable set of demands and bonus tiles that come out each turn. These, combined with what your opponents are doing and the disposition of cubes on the mancala, dictate your strategy which can be quite different from game to game. While the game doesn't exactly drip theme, each of the areas makes sense thematically.

Trajan plays like a well developed Feld game.  Feld has acquired quite a talent in putting together games that comprise mini-games in which several actions interact with each other. RATING: 7
This is a Stephan Feld point-salad euro. If you have played any of his previous titles, you probably have a general idea about how this game plays. But honestly, this may be his strongest point-salad. First, you have this mancala mechanism that creates some intensely brain-burning decisions, especially as players attempt to not only do specific actions but to activate Trajan/Bonus tiles at the same time. Combined that with 6-different actions, which are mini-games in themselves, and tons of concrete information, the decisions/possibilities seem endless. And if you somehow manage to comprehend the mancala mechanism quickly, you can recognize what other players are doing and potentially manipulate the speed of the game to hinder their progress. And finally, the game manages to support a wide range of tactical and strategic decisions. 

Ultimately, this is a point-salad game on crack. If you like Feld's designs, this is the one you should work up to.

RATING: 9.8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In Trajan players are attempting to earn glory by helping to expand Rome's power and influence.  Despite frequent criticisms to the contrary, I quite like the way this game handles its theme.  While I wouldn't call it a "thematic game" like Descent or Spartacus, I would say it's on par with something like Tigris and Euphrates (which I also think is thematically strong).

Players must influence the powerful factions and families in rome (represented buy colored wood on their mancala) to create coalitions that result in some sort of action - trading, influence in the senate, rebuilding roman neighborhoods, or conquests in the frontier.

Like many games of this era (particularly Feld's games) you get points in lots of different ways.  Each turn feels productive and gives the player a lot of choice in how to proceed. 

Manipulating the mancala is very clever and very interesting, and the beating heart of this game.  

Note: all my plays of this are 2 player -- my wife and I love it!

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Jelenleg úgy tűnik, hogy ez a játék lesz (lett) a 2011-es esseni felhozatalból a 2. legnagyobb durranás, ehhez képest nekem kicsit csalódás. Talán túl sokat vártam az értékelések miatt, meg Feld miatt, de annak ellenére nem éreztem elég ütősnek, hogy sok minden tetszik benne. El tudom képzelni, hogy másodjára nagyon bejön majd, de elsőre elmaradt a katarzis...

A játék Feld többi játékához képest bonyolult, sokkal több tényezős, nehezebben tervezhető, a rengeteg lehetőség közt szinte el lehet veszni, de eredeti ötletben számomra a Macao vagy a Notre Dame többet adott. Persze pozitívumnak is felfogható a rengeteg lehetőség (számtalan győzelmi út kínálkozik, nem érzem, hogy lenne egy tuti), de talán az illik erre a játékra, hogy "a kevesebb néha több". Már a szabály olvasásánál is felmerült bennem, hogy jóég mennyi lapka, van a bónusz, meg a bónusznak a bónusza... Játék közben ez nem tűnik ijesztőnek, mégis túl sok kicsit. A mancala-rondella nem rossz, bár az is felfogható egy külön játéknak szinte, főleg hogy a kör végére és bizonyos lapkákra is figyelni kell. Egyébként a Burgundhoz hasonlóan általában a jó és a még jobb közül lehet választani, tehát ha valaki ész nélkül hajtja végre az akciókat, annak is lesz sikerélménye, de egy kis fifikával egész durva kombók is szerezhetők (pl. a +2 akció nagyon sokat tud tejelni).
A körszámláló tetszik, és a játékosok külön tábláin nagyon jó az elrendezés, és általában véve is szép a játék!
Összességében jó, de mégis kicsit többet vártam a szerző legbonyolultabb játékától, kicsit öncélúnak érzem a sok-sok lehetőség felhalmozását, a kreativitás helyett  optimalizálási magasiskolát kaptunk...
A Burgund-dal egy szintre tettem pontszámban, a Notre Dame és a Macao mögé... A Dragonosnál viszont szerintem jobb, annak ellenére, hogy az sokkal egyedibb.
Hossza: a játékosonként fél óra nekünk nem jött be, 2 fős játék volt másfél óra, de még új volt a játék...
---
Na megvolt egy 3 fős is, kb. ugyanazt adta játékélményben, mint a 2 fős, csak kicsit hosszabban. Néhány dolgon azért jobban érződik így a szűkösség (pl. építés). Most már körvonalazódnak azok a részek, amikre mindenképp érdemes áldozni (+2 lapkák, építkezés), de még mindig úgy érzem, hogy nagyon sok módon lehet nyerni.
---
3 parti alapján úgy érzem, hogy az építés túl erős akció. A kártyás részhez kapcsolódó bónuszok pedig gyengék (kevés a 3 pont/lap). Tisztán a harci rész se túl erős. A politikai résszel talán lehet kezdeni valamit, de még nem próbáltuk ki, tisztán mennyit hozna (2+3+4+5+... azért ez elég biztató sor).
---
Számolgattam kicsit, ezek alapján az jött ki, hogy ha tisztán egy akciótípusra rámegy valaki, akkor egyértelműen a politikai akció a legerősebb (a legtöbb pontot adja, és más nem tud benne megakadályozni!!). A következő legjobb az építés, utána a trajan lapkák, majd a hajó, végül a leggyengébb a tisztán katonai akció. Önmagában a fórum akció nem jó semmire, csak töltelék akciónak jó. Mit értek tiszta stratégián? Kb. az akciók 2/3-a lehet egyféle akció, ügyes mancalázással. Egy játékban  átlagosan 28-29 akciónk van alapból (a plusz akciók ezen felül jönnek), tehát 20 azonos típusú akcióval nyugodtan lehet számolni, ha valaki egy típusra megy rá.
A játékban egyébként nagyon eltérő lehet a körök száma, nagyjából 20 a minimum és 50 a maximum körszám, játékosszámtól függően +-1.
---
Na leteszteltem élesben a szenátusi akciót, úgy, hogy az ellenfelekkel közöltem előre, hogy azt fogom csinálni: 124 pontot szereztem csak ezzel (a max. lehetséges 140-ből)... Szerintem egyértelmű, hogy ez a nyerő, sajnos ez meg is öli a játékot. Ha az ellenfelek összebeszélnek és csak arra mennek, hogy elszedjék a szenátusi akció duplázó lapkákat (azt hiszem, 3 van alapból + 2 jóker), akkor is 1-2-t szinte biztosan megszerzek, így a 100 pont elérése 99%, és ez csak a szenátusi akcióból. Ezen kívül 30-40 pont simán összekaparható, azokkal az akciókkal, amik szükség szerűen nem lehetnek szenátus akciók (kb. az akciók 1/3-a). Ráadásul a szenátusban bónuszlapkák is szerezhetők, amik szintén adnak pontokat... Mivel a többi akciótípusban a pontszerzésünk mástól is függ, ezért szerintem ha valaki nem a szerencsére és mások jóindulatára akarja bízni az eredményt, akkor a politikai akcióra megy rá, így a vége az lesz, hogy mindenki arra megy, és a legügyesebb mancalás nyer. Ha valaki nem a politikaira megy, azt a többiek simán meg tudják akadályozni az 1/3 maradék akciójukkal abban, hogy magas pontja legyen.

Még egy hiba: a térképen eddig minden játékban megszívta valaki a jobb oldali 2 tartomány közti átjárhatóság hiányát: ez elég illúzióromboló megoldás.
---
Jelenleg 4 Feld játék azonos pontszámon áll nálam, nem tudok dönteni közülük:)

RATING: 8
Stefan Feld is the genius of the unusual mechanics, and I am always fascinated by his designs.
The mancala-like action selection mechanic is at the same time brilliant, frustrating, absolutely theme-unrelated (although Trajan has some theme) and mind-boggling!
If I'd have to define it using a figure of speech, I'd say it feels like a spider's web: everytime you touch a thread somewhere, everything else will move accordingly...
I love it.
Of course, when you play Trajan you feel you're playing against the game more than versus the opponents: the sheer quantity of different permutations is too much to handle (of course after some plays this feeling vanishes, but I am not so sure...), so the game seems more a solitaire puzzle with the occasional getting in the way of another player. I absolutely need more plays to see if it cand stand multiple plays, and if the player interaction increases when the intricacies of the game become a second nature and players can better read what the opponents might do.

RATING: 7.1
A deep game that has a real learning curve. To win the game, you will have to hold your strategy and to be opportunistic to recover bonus tiles that everyone covets.

The action system, created from the awalé is original and not easy to master. It can even be frustrating to see that in a round our choice of action will be limited to a single choice.

The 30 min games by players announced are respected and we do not see the time passing.

The only negative point is that the interaction is limited to taking a tile faster than the opponents.

RATING: 7.5
I've currently only played this using a solo variant that mimics a 2p game but it works well. After multiple plays with it, I so far really enjoy Trajan. The big selling point is the mancala mechanic that determines what actions you can take and if you get any Trajan bonuses. Trying to take actions while also setting yourself up for future actions is a great puzzle. The "mini games" on the board that correspond to every action I find work pretty well and really cause to me think about what to focus on each game. After only playing CoB and Trajan from Mr. Feld, this is my favorite between those two. They both are point-salad puzzly games but this one is a bit heavier which I prefer. It's also longer to play so playing with 3 or 4 players may change how I feel for better or for worse.

RATING: N/A
1176
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Pro's.

1) The mancala and how it combines with your actions is very compelling, using it to then maximize your board actions feels great when you achieve your goals.
2) Variety of choice and how to maximize your points gain. It is often difficult to decide what is the best option yet your choice never feels invalid.
3) There is really something special about getting that 3 - 4 times action repeat combo. Managing to acquire and ship goods for many points or completing those Trajan tiles or sending out your military on campaign.
4) Very well balanced for multiple player counts.

Con's:

1) The game is not thematic, but at the same time, I'm very thankful for the little theme that is actually there.
2) Sometimes it seems like it is better to get easy points then to plan for a grand combo. Particularly if you have to prevent the negative points for not fulfilling the people's demands. This can then derail your entire super combo plan as it destroys the mancala you may have been planning for.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
It's Macao redux and I mean that in the worst possible way.  Another game where Feld restricts your actions with some kind of gimmicky, arcane wheel combined with a bunch of non-interacting disparate game systems.  And of course there is a penalty for not doing something.  His games are so trite and predictable at this point.

I really must stress that the entire game is manipulating the stupid action wheel.  Everyone's heads are down all the time studying their wheel and trying to get it to do what they want.  Player interaction is minimal and since the actions don't really affect each other, there is not much strategy.

Don't buy the hype, there are better dry mechanical Euros like the recently released Tzolkin that provide a more interesting puzzle.

RATING: 7
I've enjoyed this game each time I've played, and the more I play the better it becomes as the strategies start to emerge a bit more clearly. Players need to balance their influence across a number of spheres, and time their actions to get the most benefit. The 'mancala' style action selection mechanism is clever and more than just a gimmick - more forgiving and more flexible than a rondel, but can still leave you unable to do the action you need most unless you sequence your actions quite carefully. This makes for quite a tense game. Good solid euro, not outstanding but happy to play this one.

RATING: N/A
Only one play so far...

Trajan suffers from one major drawback that makes me not necessarily want to play it again - a lack of connection between the gameplay and the theme.  The gameplay itself has multiple mechanisms being crammed together for almost no reason whatsoever except to make the game strategic.  That works in abstract games for me, but not so much in non-abstracts.

However, the actual game strategies are there and it wins points for that.  I just miss the mechanics to theme connection.

RATING: 10
Long rules explanation and setup time, but once you start playing the game flows beautifully.  Fast turns and the game plays more quickly than you would think.  The mancala and Trajan tiles mechanic is awesome and keeps the game from feeling like an optimization problem.  Lots of ways to get points and all are fun and seem to be pretty balanced so you feel like you have a lot of options and that you are making some progress.  Criticizing this for lack of theme is silly, it's comparable theme-wise to most euros- it vaguely feels like you're managing a very abstract Roman empire.  Anyway, the game is great and extremely fun.

RATING: 7
Trajan has one of the most interesting action mechanics I've ever seen in a game. That being said, it takes some adjustment to get used to. You also have a relatively small amount of actions throughout the game, so there is a need to make them count! One slip-up can really cost you! This is a game where you SHOULD get better with each play. There seems to be a variety of winning strategies and lots of replay value. It's a very solid euro!

RATING: 6
2-4 | (4) | 90 min | 12+ | Stefan Feld
Trajan is probably one of the most intriguing designs of Feld thanks to the mancala mechanism at its core. I fear, however, that you cannot have enough control over the mancala to interfere with your opponents' moves at the same time you are pursuing your own agenda. Other than that, Trajan is a typical Feld's design: a race in which you can score points in dozens of different ways. This time there are no rats or enemies: the difficulty lies in the restrictions imposed by the mancala. More plays needed before giving it a final rating.

RATING: 10
Wow, mind blown. I literally had mancala dreams the night after playing this for the first time. We also had a good hour-long discussion afterwards, which usually means a game is good (though not everyone at the table agreed).

Mechanically, it's wonderful. Like Macao, this is one of the only games in which I can actually plan four or five turns in advance, the mancala (like the wind rose) serving only as a mnemonic aid to help visualize my strategy. It's such a simple idea, and actually makes the game more accessible IMO (as opposed to games like Chess in which planning multiple moves in advance often requires years of practice). Yes, the theme is nonexistent: like the mancala, the Roman setting is there to help players visualize and remember the different scoring mechanisms, which are engaging and fascinating by themselves. 

If I had one complaint, it would be that being unable to fulfill Rome's demands doesn't seem to be punishing enough: it is perfectly possible to win the game by largely ignoring the demands (or most of them). On the other hand, consistently fulfilling them might give you enough of a lead that you could win this way as well. There are so many ways to earn points that you could try a dozen different strategies, all of which seem equally valid. If this is a "point salad," then please, give me seconds!

RATING: 7
Really neat action planning game where, due to the mancala-like system, you often have to choose between what action you need now, and what actions you may want access to in the near future.

Other than the novelty and coolness of the action system, it's the fairly typical Feld point salad game, where a billion things score you points in various ways, and you're trying to most efficiently capitalize on those various point scoring methods.  There are definitely ways to focus on certain point systems, but you'll likely end up using all of them at some time or another throughout the game.

Despite all of the rules surrounding all of the point structures, the game actually ends up being quite a simple in terms of what you do on a turn.  The main heaviness comes from trying to decide what to do from the many options presented.  For this reason, definitely avoid this game with AP-prone players, as it would likely turn the game into a nightmare.  However, with a reasonably quick group, this game is a fun one.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
 trajan is such a neat puzzle of a game even if the theme feels a little bland.  i've noticed the advantage of going first in this game especially if you take a double trajan action tile early on.  If a player starts collecting multiple 9 pt trajan tiles, it is hard to counter this later on.  But there are numerous ways to score in this game and good players can stifle this winning strategy.

RATING: 2
Trajan does just about everything that I hate in a boardgame. It is totally unthematic (even when compared to classic-era euros, which I'd much rather play),  it has a tedious setup, it smooshes together a bunch of uninteresting little subsystems into one big uninteresting system, it is wearingly dry, and senselessly overwrought.

And it isn't like I'm a Feld hater either. Bruges and Castles of Burgundy are both pretty good. They aren't exactly my thing, but I've enjoyed playing both of them. Trajan, unlike those two, is cold and calculating and heartless. 

I will never play it again. Never. And I will be really happy when this style of Eurogame goes out of fashion -  which the nature of the hobby being what it is, shouldn't be more than a few years away - and we get a return of the more straightforward and thematic Euros of a couple of decades ago.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Wow... my head hurts.

Trajan, along with Ora et Labora, is a game that really gets my gears turning. There's so much going on that every turn presents a number of different decisions to make. 

The heart of the game is that mancala mechanic, and it's a fascinating "engine" for the game. It's complex to manage, but yet surprisingly easy to pick up. I expected to struggle with it more than I did. The game also went by more quickly than I expected.

This is definitely Feld, so if you're a fan of his stuff, you'll like this, and if you're not, you won't. The "meeting demand" phase adds a bit of hardship, but we're not talking In the Year of the Dragon or even Notre Dame brutality here. And if I look hard enough, I even see a little bit of a theme peeking through!

I'll be playing this more for sure, as other fellow gamers appear to like this as well.

Update: 2nd play online, and I'm loving this. More than any other Euro game, this is the one I want to play at least 5+ times to uncover its depths. What I love about this game is that I find myself looking ahead 2, 3, or even four turns. That's a different feel than some of the more highly tactical games I've played that prevent planning ahead. Trajan allows you to set out to try and do something, and then figure out the right path. It's a blast so far and I'm really amped about this game.

Update: ...and so, I've played the game 10+ times now, and I can safely trade away my copy. Only two of those plays were of my actual copy, and the physical game simply won't see much play in my circles.

After 10+ plays, I still freakin' love that mancala action selection, but the rest of the game is bone dry regarding theme. Castles of Burgundy has more theme (weee!!). The other annoyance I have with the game is the distribution of those extra action tiles. My group has learned to abuse the X2 and the forum to quickly strip away any action tiles for others. With less than four players, it's even worse if you commit to a strategy but then never see the extra action tiles for that strategic path. 

I'll play it but I don't need to own it.

RATING: 8
Fascinating combination of mostly disparate systems. If ever there were a VP soup game, this is it. While I don't personally have a problem with such games, it's doubtful this will rise above an 8 for me for one very clear reason: it's nearly impossible to pay attention to what everyone else is doing. It takes significant processing looking at players' individual player mats in front of them to even figure out what their options are. In practice, for the folks I play with at least, it's intractable. So what other players will do becomes a large random element. At least in the short-term. I still think it's interesting enough to be "great".

RATING: 7
My first response to Trajan when it came out was 'Feld, skip'. Feld is about constructing clever mechanisms and exploiting them. He's made a number of games which are about creating point snowball engines and exploiting them: that is rarely my style of game. But in Die Burgen von Burgund he has shown to be capable of creating good puzzle games, and after having played Trajan in a more relaxed setting at the request of my partner (who harbours less prejudice against designers), I find myself liking this design too. Trajan is rather like Burgen in the sense that the players are solving a point puzzle rather than creating an engine and milking it for all it's worth; but the Mancala-style rondel adds an interesting twist to the story in that what the players can do is bereft of randomness whatsoever apart from the initial distribution of tokens in the cups. Also, Trajan plays a lot quicker than Burgen, also at greater player numbers, and that adds to its fluidity. Finally, the physical appearance of Trajan is better than Burgen with its washed-out colours and thin alea-cardboard. 

I mentioned 'puzzle game' above, and that is really all you need to know. In my collection and its peripheries you can find titles like Shipyard, Cities, 20th Century and of course Die Burgen von Burgund (and perhaps, a smidgen of Dungeon Lords too)—if you like these titles, you can safely assume that you'll like Trajan too. If not, then you'd probably want skip this title. So, what is not to like? Well, that is [i]is[/i] a puzzle game, as paradoxically as that may sound. In Trajan there is little in the way of interacting with other players. Oh, there can be quite a few little pinpricks where player A takes a desirable resource just before player B can, but that is all there is to it. There is no feeling of being clever in this regard, no need to come up with a tricky and inescapable tactical combination of sorts. Also the central timing rondel is a little cumbersome, but with some experience it works nicely.

But the fact that it is an unashamed puzzle game makes me hesitant to increase the rate beyond 7, although I really like Trajan, and cannot, for the time being, envisage it leaving my collection. I expect more of the [i]game[/i] of a title when it is awarded an 8 or higher. I need to mull on this for a while while playing seamlessly at all player numbers.

Those whom love the crunch of theme, story and adventure will find none here and I'd suggest Lords of Waterdeep instead.

RATING: 8.5
This game makes your mind creak from internal tension in a way that few other games achieve. The mancala mechanic is the game, not just a gimmick. As others have commented, this does a tremendous job of rendering the bureaucracy of the Roman Empire. There is constant tension between long and short term decision making, a constant need to consider whether you should reprioritise or march on ahead, and it is so hard to know what's best to do. And in amongst all this, you have to try and handle your internal administration, which requires planning out c. 4 moves in advance, where each move could damage the next one, and the colours and number of pieces you move can have dramatic implications. Incredible, but so taxing.

RATING: 9
Neat euro style multi-player game.  Trajan uses a cool game mechanic for determining one's action on their turn.  This game mechanic forces players to do their allocation and plan a turn or two in advance.  The game is a constant tug of war for trying to gain victory points, avoid the pitfalls of coming up short at certain points of the game lest you take negative victory points and work towards collecting certain tiles and taking control of certain geographical areas of the Roman Empire, all while attempting to avoid other player's attempts to thwart you.

RATING: 6
I can see why people like this: manipulating the mancala to set up bonuses as well as the actions you want is a fun puzzle. It just didn't do anything special for me. At least, nothing that Castles and Aquasphere didn't already do. 

Trajan just has too much going on - too many chits, too many options, and everything really just gets you a handful of points no matter what.  You end up spending a lot of time setting up and maintaining pieces of the board that you'll never get to (very similar to Great Western Trail, which we also disliked).

Castles does the "bonus action" thing in a simpler and more satisfying manner - and you build something too!  Aquasphere does the pre-planning thing in a much more transparent manner, and is far more interactive (Trajan is just a race, while Aquasphere has competition for things on the board, as well as a more significant area control aspect)

RATING: 7
The Feld of all Felds. A showcase for what I dislike about his games: several sub games combined, all using unconnected mechanisms, constant scoring by everyone no matter what they do, and at the end of the game someone wins but couldn't tell you why. And no connection whatsoever to the theme they've hung on it. I can't imagine that the person who designed the box cover had ever played the game. 

It's not like I don't enjoy playing his games--they aren't *bad* games. They just all have the same feel to me: clever, soulless machines. You play one a few times until you've figured out how the mechanisms fit together, and then the next one comes along.

Update: I really disliked this the first time I played for all the reasons above, but recently played again and...rather enjoyed it. The mandala mechanism is actually pretty fun. But everything I said stands.

RATING: 8.6
As in all the others best known Stephan Feld´s games, the theme is almost absent, there are lots of ways to gain points and there is (almost) always something useful to do when reaching each one´s turn.
The basic mechanic of the game is a kind of "round mancala" that puts your brain to work a lot but it´s also one of the assets of the game.
The set up requires a little work and time, as some knowledge of the game rules is required to make the game a little more swift, but the strategic experience is well worthwhile, as in others games by Feld.
The game can however deliver some punishment, if a player doesn´t manage to make the right action at the right time or if the (random) people´s demands don´t match the tiles we invested before...

RATING: 7
Trajan is much more streamlined that it appears at first. The mechanics flow together smoothly and there are many, many possible paths to pursue.

However, the theme is only OK. Mostly, you're simply trying to cobble together a series of tokens, cards, tiles and moves which will combine for maximum points. There's much more of a solving-a-puzzle feel than a ruling-an-empire feel.

(Don't get me wrong, the puzzle aspect of this game--including the mancala-like action board--is actually pretty good... it's just a bit dry.)

If you don't already have Macao (also by Stefan Feld), absolutely get Macao instead. If you have Macao and love it, you'll probably find Trajan worth getting, too. They're not really all that similar, but Feld's designs do have a distinctive character to them.

RATING: N/A
[2015-03-02 First Impression]
To put it short: Trajan blew me away. It's been some time since a game this heavy impressed me that much and made me want to play it again immediately. Complexity is the name of the game, everything seems to be connected with everything else and it's easy to get distracted by all the shiny bits. On top of it all, you have the mancala that controls your actions and currently I'm really bad at managing it. 

But I guess, Trajan will only get better once you figure out the mancala . While your first game will see you struggling for the action you want to do, I guess further games will reveal a lot more strategic layers. How do I set up my mancala initially? Which free actions will I hunt after? What are my opponents up to and how can they interfere with my plans? How can I manage my mancala so that I'll be the start player in the next quarter so that I'll get first dips on the new shiny tiles? So much to think about and I'm not even remotely close to reaching this level of play.

I was thinking about Trajan for some days after I played it and I'm really looking forward to get it to the table again as soon as possible. But this might prove to be a problem as I think that this game won't work well with my regular gaming group and this has something to do with the kind of game Trajan is. Like most Feld games, I think Trajan will be enjoyed most if you value mechanics more than theme.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5.5
The Mancala mechanism for action selection is extremely clever, if somewhat brain melting, although it does nothing really to connect the game to it's theme, something which applies to nearly all of the rest of the game - only the military campaigning aspect has any engagement with the subject matter. 

For some the paper-thin theme won't matter, they just crave clever ways to get VP's, but personally I like a bit more narrative to a game and this just doesn't quite do it enough. However, the Mancala mechanic and the military campaigning component to the game do add enough interest to this to lift it slightly above the other cube pushers and I would play it again and might pick up a copy if they were giving it away.

I subsequently read the following comment by Paul Lister (MR Boardgameguru) and it perfect;ly sums up my feeling on the game [i]"...game felt ...  solitaire ...with (only) a little competition for the military spaces, otherwise, players focused on own rondel. Too many point scoring routes for competition to be very meaningful. Moreover, the linked actions feel like a math exercise. Admirable, but tedious."[/i]

RATING: 8
After 1 play, starting this out at an 8.  This is a high ranking for me for a Euro after 1 play.   I really enjoyed the decisions.  Moving the barrels mancala-style was cool mechanic, but it was a little cludgey.  "I need this color barrel here for this Trajan tile.  And I need one over here.  Wait, where have I put my barrels?  Oh and I'm going to get another tile soon.  It will go here, so I need to redo this...I can't remember what I've put down already!"
I like the variety the game offers, but I'm wondering if after repeated plays if there are some overpowered combos.  If so, that could make being first at the beginning of each quarter important.  And that is not something which can be manipulated easily in a strategic sense.

Updated: from 8 to 9 after 6 plays.  Really enjoying it.  Yeah the theme is weak, but it gives you some really tough choices and is fun.

Lowered back to a an 8 after 14 plays.  It's a great game, but I'm not finding myself wanting to request it all the time any longer.  Maybe the strategy is played out for me.

RATING: 7
Players are Roman nobles in the time of Trajan, taking various actions and claiming victory points.  Busy game where players each run their own mancala board, with six pits and twelve stones.  They empty a pit and seed the stones, and take the action associated with their destination pit.  Actions include senate influence, shipping goods, construction, military campaigning, and so on.  These actions are interwoven, give you points, and can potentially kick off a chain of bonus actions.  Trajan tiles are also bonuses that ring your pits, and trigger if you can get the correct coloured stones in the target pit.  Busy game, takes a while to explain and get your head around the mancala mechanism, but plays surprisingly quickly once you're up and running.  As with many Feld games, a weird mechanic drives the action, and tons of victory points are out there to be had, as well as penalties for the unwary.  Thematically questionable, but nice and meaty without overdoing it.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very interesting, brainburning game of resource management with a mancalla rondel action thingy...
Game as a lot of potential since there are many, many ways of making points. Lots of different actions and things to watch for, but not too hard to grok completely. Can be overwhelming at first but i'm sure that with time, you can plot a reasonable strategy at first and adjust as the game progress. the amount of player interaction is medium to low, with the players unable to directly affect each other, but rather interact by taking away resources that they wanted.
My main beef with the game is the rules, with many little holes that need to be clarified (ie, when shipping goods, do the cards go away? if not, then what stops me from shipping them multiple times?)
Rating will likely go up with more play and more rule clarifications (keep in mind that we did play with 2nd ed rules and we felt that there were still holes to be patched)

RATING: 8
I am not typically a fan of games with this many different, disjointed ways to score.  You've got at least three independent set collections going on, with a area majority contest in the senate to boot.  Yowza.

However, it all manages to hang together really well, and is very satisfying to play.  The various scoring methods are very well balanced, so you do have a lot of choices as to how you want to play.  Specializing or spreading around also seems equally viable, depending on what your opponents are doing.  This plays well with 2, but I'd like to try it with 4, because I think this will make things even more dynamic, and give the Senate more importance.

This is largely a heads-down game as players study their mancala, but you do need to pay attention to your opponents because there is competition for everything on the board.  

The game is beautifully produced, and is another game that makes me mad at the crappy production Alea gave to the otherwise excellent Castles of Burgundy.

RATING: 9.8
Stefen Feld is a genius. Bravo for making so many fun games.

Beautifully designed game. So much fun and so much that can be done depending on how you planned to move your Mancala type of wheel.

So much you want to do and sometimes it feels like in so little time before the season ends (4 rounds). There are so many ways to victory that all can have access to. I love that. So much fun. Play this, you won't regret it.

elvarázsolt.
Kicsit a Caylus élmény jött vissza: anno mikor megjelent és megismertem ott éreztem hasonlót: sok út, választhatok, áttervezhetek, ha keresztbe tesznek, ha netán picit hibázom helyrehozhatom.
Nekünk hosszú volt, de azért mert közben volt gofri sütés, és apró-cseprő gyermekek pesztrálása az első óra után. (4 kölök, 3,5 évesnél kisebbek)
De így is mindenki élvezte.
Normál körülmények közt valszeg belefér 1,5-2 órába a játék.
(Kivéve a sokat gondolkodókkal :-))
Ugyanakkor szabály magyarázat kb 15 perc volt, és igazából az első 2-3 forduló után már mindenki átlátta, és elkezdtünk komolyabban előre tervezni. /Amit persze időnként borít 1-1 jó lehetőség/
A 4 negyedév sem feltétlen egyforma hosszú: egy idő után felhalmozódik a "bab" a tálban, és akkor gyorsan lepörög egy-egy forduló.
Szóval nekem a nagy kedvenc kategóriába sorolódott.
Csak azért nem 10-es, mert kíváncsi vagyok, h tényleg egyforma sok út van-e: első ránézésre úgy tűnik igen, de ehhez több játék kell, h tényleg így van-e
Na meg nagy bánatom, h csak max 4 személyes :-(
De mindent összevetve: végig lekötő, szinte holtidő mentes, előretervezhető, de hirtelen lehetőség lecsapási lehetőség, koncentrálós, figyelős, izgalmas
Ja és ami még fontos: szép, és jól átlátható a tábla, és a játékosok táblája is.
Update: második játék ugyanazzal a csapattal, Továbbra is izgalmas érdekes: és most teljesen más úton nyertem meg :-9.
Nagyon jó kis kombókat lehet csinálni benne -és ez több féle:
pl. munkásos trajanus lapka, munkás akció duplázó, és +2-es hozzá: így nagyon jól lehet akár 2-3 épületet is egyszerre felhúzni az építési területen. Ugyanez megvan a katonai megoldásra is, a kikötőre is -ezt még nem próbáltam, azt hiszem legközelebb erre megyek rá :-)-

RATING: 6
I don't really know what can be said about Trajan that hasn't already been said: various Feldian subsystems that results in a point salad style game with a Mancala action selection method.  I didn't think I would like this game since at the end of a round (which consist of multiple player turns), you need to meet the objectives of the people or suffer negative consequences.  This is one of my least favorite aspects of Agricola and Le Havre.  Surprisingly, the point salad-style of gameplay makes it much easier for me to compare actions that help provide needs (avoid negative points) versus other scoring methods (giving positive points).  The combination of puzzling the Mancala action selection system with Trajan tiles is really the main thing that makes the game enjoyable for me. I really like the cover, but find the rest of the graphic design to be functional, but mediocre.

RATING: 6
Trajan has a fairly unique action scheme, but it does feel like they gave up a little on it. The number of actions seems pretty random, since most players will prefer to make a good move then worry about how that affects their portion of ending the turn. The game also seems to be broken up into several subgames, in which you can ahive points. There is politics for point bonus cards, the city for monopoly bonuses and military for area control bonuses and discovery perks. 

Overall the game is decent, just a hair to long and the only major complaint is the general lack of theme at all.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Another Feld game of limiting choices through novel mechanics. This time it's mancala. This is the first Feld game in a long time (maybe ever) that I've been satisfied with the components - both size and artwork. Another thing that I really like about the Feld designs is the high point count. High points help with differentiation and selection between two actions and there's also that 'short chapters in a book' feeling of accomplishment when you round the 100 point line. More thematic than most Feld games, but that really isn't saying too much. 

Thinking more about this game, I would say its closest relative is Die Burgen von Burgund. Instead of dice limiting your actions, you are limited by your choices on the mancala wheel. There is an increased game complication and an obvious increase in length. There may or may not be an increase in game control in Trajan since you can modify your dice rolls in dBvB with workers and acquired technology tiles. Pretty much every system in Trajan is more complicated when compared to its closest system in dBvB, an obvious exception is the combos created by castle and building tiles in dBvB. 

My first impression as a Feld fan is that both of these games are worth owning. However, if you are not as diehard a Feld fan, you may want to consider one over the other. Your preference of game length, complication, and presence or absence of dice should guide your decision. If theme is an absolute dealbreaker for you I would suggest Trajan.

RATING: 7
While Terra mystica helped me to realize that I am more of a ludologist than I realized, this game caused me to realize that I can also be very shallow and superficial. Because quite frankly, I love the games mechanics (which are fascinating, deep, complicated, rich and rewardin) the board is so ugly!  I mean, Stefan Feld is a prolific designer, why not get an equally great graphic designer???  The box art looks fantastic, and when I think of Rome I think of amazing bold beautiful colors. Amazing architecture with crimson buttresses  set against a backdrop of one of the worlds most gorgeous landscapes... And then I open the box and it looks like... Well, this is bgg- check out the unfiltered images.  It would be great if you just stared at the box the whole time but your focus is on one of two equally ugly boards that do not look like room. The game doesn't even feel like Rome (which isn't that big of a deal). but if I'm going to be sitting staring at a board and thinking heavily about it for a couple hours I want something better to look at.  It felt like winning a dream vacation to Italy's capital and then spending the entire time working in an active leper colony.game design a 9, components and appearance a 3.

RATING: 9.5
This game is amazing.  It ranks among his finest works (Year of the Dragon, Macao, Bora Bora, & Castles of Burgundy).  Length of time takes around 2.5 hours w/4 players, but is so gripping the time flies by very quickly.  I love the Mancala action circle system.

A few small caveats prevent this game from reaching a perfect 10 for me though:

1) I'm not overly fond of the era-end mechanic.  It seems odd, and while it may be balanced it doesn't feel that way.

2) I would have preferred less commodities. Having less, but worth more points for the senator tiles (and maybe only 1 face up draw pile) may have created a better system.  I suppose the varying amounts is important for the balance of pairs vs. different cards.  This aspect of the game does not match the greatness of the other mechanisms (i.e., the building sector and the Trajan tiles).

3) Finally, the military path seems to be slightly weaker than the other options for scoring VP's and collecting tiles.

But in the end, this game's a keeper and Stefan Feld surely did it again - another masterpiece!  Grade: A

RATING: 10
Rules Complexity: :star::star::star::halfstar::nostar:
Strategic Depth: :star::star::star::star::halfstar:
Luck Factor :halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Negotiation Factor: :nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Amount of Downtime: :star::star::halfstar::nostar::nostar:
Fiddliness: :star::halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Replayability: :star::star::star::star::star:
Component Quality: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Player Interaction: :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:
"Take That" Conflict: :halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Theme Integration: :star::star::halfstar::nostar::nostar:

This was my favorite new Essen game from BGG.CON 2011. So many paths to victory, and so many points available to grab. The action selection "mancala" mechanic is so cool and accounts for so many deep and agonizing decisions. This mechanic is reminiscent of Finca, but the game is very heavy compared to that family game. There are several people I would never want to play this game with because there is plenty of opportunity for analysis paralysis. Rules will probably take a half-hour to explain, but each turn is a small enough, bite-sized piece of action that it is not overwhelming. I can hardly wait for a North American publisher to pick this gem up!

RATING: 9.25
2012 birthday gift! I'm getting more and more satisfied with Stefan Feld's designs... I still don't try tis one but I'm pretty sure it'll gonna be one of my favourite games. After give it a try I'll post here my opinions and rating.

2012/11/12 UPDATE: Feld is becoming one of my favourite game designers of all time and Trajan is, IMHO, the best Feld game so far! The use of mancala system is outstanding. The game is fast paced (as with other Feld's designs), but is very deep (deeper, in fact, than Castles of Burgundy). Trajan is a tactical and strategic game (I'd say 50% of each). There's a lot of posibilities on this game to choose from, but it's game system makes feel it lighter and easier than it really is (wich is something I love on Feld's games: that feeling of "easy to learn but hard to master"). For now my rating is a solid 10. Let's see if in the future this changes or not, even though, I don't see it very probably to happen.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Trajan shares a lot with Castles of Burgundy. So much, in fact, that I'm going to claim it's pretty much the same game with just the action selection mechanism exchanged for the mancala-style rondel. There are a few other minor differences (CoB has a stronger spatial aspect, for example) but in both games the focus is essentially on solving a micro-optimization puzzle. Perhaps not surprisingly it feels rather similar to Macao in many respects, too, again with another way to select actions.

Macao at least has a bit of uncertainty and some sort of risk/reward or short/long-term tradeoffs whereas Trajan is just straight point-mongering from start to finish. I have no idea why people get excited by playing solo mancala in order to compete in various mini games, but apparently they do.

RATING: 7
Der Ludo Centurio Stephanus Feldus verbaut sein Talent für geschmierte Verzahn-Denk-Puzzles im guten alten römischen Spielethema. Im Zentrum der Macht steht in TRAJAN ein schöner Mancala-Mechanismus, der sechs Aktionen umfasst, die sich dann in diversen zusätzlichen auslösbaren Boni-Aktioni wiederfinden. Hier! Da! Dort! Überall lassen sich Züge optimieren, Siegpunkte sammeln und wieder abziehen, falls man die Bedürfnisse des dekadenten Volkes nach Brot und Spielen nicht erfüllt. Die Kritiker von Feld werden in TRAJAN problemlos fündig: viele Stellschrauben am Streitwagen und Siegpunktgesammel an vielen Stellen lassen den Spieler zum Eichhörnchen werden. Und seine Fans werden glücklich sein: TRAJAN ist ein funktionierende römische Einheit, Schönheit für das Auge, haptisch in seiner Plättchenflut, üppig im klemmigen Handeln. Der Autorus dieser Zeilus reiht sich ein in die Legionen der Zufriedenen.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Here's another Stefan Feld game that feels like a signature game if only because it has his style all over it! The rondel/mancala mechanic is very clever and I did feel like I was analyzing it constantly to try to make the best moves. And it was interesting to think about it and plan to use it optimally. The actions that are activated through the rondel are less interesting and frankly feel very disconnected. I think Feld is at his most brilliant when he can take very smart ideas and still tie them together in a seemingly abstract/Euro game. I don't get sucked into Trajan the way I do with Luna, nor do I feel everything comes together like CoB or ITYotD.  It's a much more impressive game than Macao, but I still walk away with the feeling that the parts just didn't come together as a whole the way I wanted them do. A good game (and fascinating design as always) but slightly underwhelmed by the experience.

RATING: 7
[b]Trajan[/b] [i]Stefan Feld 2011[/i]
Top 100 material, after Burgundy, Macao, Notre Dame and In the year of the Dragon this game proves that Stefan Feld is the designer of the moment. A mancala system, many mini games that alltogether forms a puzzle that works flawlessly and deserves a very high position in the rankings. Eurogame at its best, a kind of rondel-mancala game that offers many different paths to victory. Trajan is one of the best games of 2011. Sure it lacks theme like most Feld´s games, but the mechanics are really good and though not very innovative, they havent been used in many eurogames.

RATING: 9
I'm a huge fan of Stefan Feld, have about all of his games, with Burgundy and Macao being the favorites. I had of course the highest expectations for Trajan while patiently waiting for it for almost half a year. It is finally here and so far i only played one game. Here are some early impressions.. Well, to sum it up, I'm slightly underwhelmed. Here is why:

- It is indeed his most complex game, a bit too complex for my taste. Also a bit too long. I do feel however that it will get easier/faster after repeated plays.. if I can get it to the table!
- The mancala felt a bit too uncontrollable and frustrating during this 1st game. I hope it will get better!
- Replayability may not be as good as some of Stefan's other games, because the same materials are used in every game and setup is pretty much fixed.
- The game is too mechanical and disconnected from theme, even for Feld. It probably doesn't help that I don't care for the Roman theme, to put it mildly. In fact I'm sick and tired of all the forums, senates and laurels, to put it less mildly.

Even despite of these flaws, you can see I can't help but rate Trajan a 9. I still enjoyed it more than %90 of mediocre stuff I played this year. I think it will only get better.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Early impressions are that this is Stefan Feld's best game. The mancala-like action wheel does a great job of creating interesting multi-dimensional decisions for the players. Those who, like me, enjoy puzzle-like qualities in games will appreciate this new mechanism. Matching the colors of the beads in order to earn Trajan tiles adds that delicious extra layer that makes the game so challenging.

The rest of the game is less important, but seems to provide the necessary level of balance and variety, and perhaps just enough interconnectedness to keep Trajan from feeling hodgepodge. As with several of Feld's games (especially Rum & Pirates) Trajan feels like walking through a carnival moving from one mini-game to another. Each mini-game has a little bit of urgency attached to it at different times in the game, such that the specific sequence of performing the actions is important. Setting up these efficient sequences on the mancala wheel is nerdy fun.

I do wonder about the game's staying power, since it displays all of the features that typically keep me from loving Feld's games: disjointed, overly mechanical, and not interactive enough. But two plays in, I'm loving it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Trajan is a very good game by Stefan Feld, the winner is the player with the most victory point.  There is a lot of ways to score VP in this game!

On is turn, a player most choose one of his plate and take all the action marker in it and drop one of them in clockwise order in each of the next plate ("Awale" style).  The colors of the marker matter because you need a combinaison of 2 colors to activate a Trajan bonus tile.
The tray where you put your last marker is the target tray and you will be able to do the action specific to this tray.  The actions are :
- Seaport (take cards or discard them to gain VP)
- Forum (take a tile in the forum section of the board)
- Military (move your leader or legionnaires in different provinces for tile or VP)
- Trajan (take a Trajan tile)
- Senate (gain VP and maybe a bonus card if you're the most advance at the senate)
- Construction (move worker to take construction tile that give VP)

At the end of a quarter year, each player have to meet the people's demands or they will lose VP.  Also, the leader at the senate choose a card that will give him bonus VP at the end of the game, the player that is second take the remaining card and flip it (the bonus will be less for him).
The game end after 4 quarter, the winner is the player with the most VP after the final scoring.

The "Awale" system for choosing action is something I haven't seen before.  It takes some time to get use to it and be able to do the action you really want.  The bonus for having the right colors in the right cup is really nice, it need some calculation to get!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
I really enjoyed this game. There is a lot of ways to earn victorypoints (typical for Stefan Feld games), which makes it hard to guess who is ahead during the game and keeps the game interesting. 

The main mechanism is to move tokens of different colors in 6 bowls, dropping one in every subsequent bowl and doing the action at the last bowl a token is dropped. This system kind of reminds of the rondel, but with the added touch of the tiles you try to complete with these moves. 

The crux of the game is to have as many turns as possible where you can do multiple things, using the double tokens, the trajan tiles and the tools. 
All six options seem quite balanced and equally viable in gaining points or preparing for a better move.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A mix of several worker placement mechanics, plus set building.  The "bowl" can be a brain burner, as it forces you to plan a few (3) turns ahead.

Possibility of action combos with Trajan and Building Tiles.  Will you be going for the bonus action, or pick up that Building tile which will hopefully allow you to have a set of 3 or 4 at the end of the game (and score a bonus 10 or 20 points)?

Some interesting decisions, and you can't really be blocked, although other players can pick up a title you were looking to get.

The "people's demand" is a nice clean mechanic which forces you to allocate a few actions in order to secure the needed tiles (forum or military) or Trajan permanent tiles (which are only worth 1 VP but will end up saving you time/actions).

The "time clock" allows for 2, 3 or 4 player configurations.

In the end, it's all about scoring points.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

The bonus tiles give you some interesting objectives to shoot for but certainly don't dictate the course you have to take. The game plays well with two, three or four players.

RATING: 5
The latest example in the ultra-complicated Euro trend. Euros used to do one or two things really well, now they try to do ten or a dozen things not so well. The Mancala bit is so good, but everything else about this game kind of rubs me the wrong way. There's no elegance here, just a big pile of scoring mechanisms that all work in their own unnecessarily convoluted ways. Most of them bear no relationship to each other, and it seems like you can just pick a couple scoring possibilities and ignore the others. The timing mechanism is a little too fiddly, and since it's not particularly interesting there's a good chance it will be forgotten from time to time. There is some true depth, though, in the board movement, wherein you can do some real planning. I would love to see this in a stripped down game (though not stripped all the way down to Mancala) with fewer actions and special scoring things to remember.

Please, game designers! Edit! Cut out the chaff and just give me the wheat.

RATING: 8.25
Wow! After one play, perhaps my favorite Feld game.

The mancala driven actions ARE (as advertised) a lot to think about, but the play seems to drive along fairly quickly despite that, especially once the bins start to collect larger numbers of color tokens.  I very much liked that way that player bin selection could drive the game at a faster or a slower pace- the element of some control definitely added some appeal for me.

The art and production values are stellar, nearly perfect, and hide the fact that this is not a particularly Roman mechanism and that the theme is a mask for a fascinating game engine.  If you don't like mancala, this may not be for you, but it's not as ridiculously brain-bending as advertised (e.g., certainly less painful for me than Agricola.)

In some ways this is also a less-painful Feld design; while there are quarterly demand rounds which can deliver a large pain in the VP, there are also plentiful ways to acheive VP, and to collect needed resources, so the game doesn't feel as tight as say, In the Year of the Dragon.

But I can easily see this as a top-50, or even a top-25 game.  It reminded me of Goa in that there are many ways to succeed, but a mix of careful planning and opportunistic acquisition is needed to finish first.

Based on a 1st solo 3-player game, I am starting this at an 8.25, with definite room to go higher.

So far, everything about this game appeals to me; and what more do we truly ask a of good/great game?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.25
Definitely a point salad style game, but that's Feld's style.  

There is so much going on in this game and I love it.  The main driving force of the game is a mancala style portion of your player board.  There are 6 actions and you can get points via all the actions.  On top of that you can get bonus actions/points if you match up certain colors on your mancala wheel with bonus tiles you have assigned on it.  

It sounds and looks like a confusing game but once you play a round of it you'll understand how everything fits together.  

This is a great mid-heavy weight Euro game.
