RATING: 9
(03/21/2014) Comment based on one play.

This is by far Stephen Feld's best of the various "point salad" games I've played--[thing=25554][/thing], [thing=70512][/thing], and [thing=84876][/thing]. It is a clever blending of familiar mechanisms from other games, along with a brilliant implementation of the mancala mechanism, seen in [thing=1970][/thing].

This game is a step towards a more deterministic point salad game, rather then the stochastic CoB, where the action choices were randomly generated by dice. Instead, the mancala acts as sort of a "rondel" (as found in [thing=19600][/thing]) hybrid, both allowing a selection of one of six actions--the last pebble sowed selects the action--and pick of a "Trajan" tile, if the conditions are met. Although the mancala seems chaotic and random, there is no luck--the player has complete control over the set of possible future actions, as well as the sequencing of them. Further, the number of "pebbles" sowed serves as the game clock, moving the game along the four sub-quarters of a quarter of a year, for a full year. A number of smaller moves can result in more turns per sub-quarter, but there will eventually be an acceleration in the game, when a player eventually has all or most of their pebbles clustered in a bowl or two.

As with his other point salad games, it seems that almost every action will result in a gaining of VP. The challenge and appeal of the game is in the search of maximizing VP per action or trading off less VP for a more lucrative payoff later in a combination of actions.

The VP landscape for two of the six action types is randomly generated at the beginning of the game, but is held relatively fixed for the rest of the game. The map of Europe has regions with known VP values, but has randomly assigned bonus tiles in each space. A sequence of military actions can first get the bonus tile through movement of the leader, followed by placement of a legion to secure the VP for that space. There is also a 4x5 grid of Rome, with randomly assigned building tiles, each with a VP value. A sequence of build actions can result in placement of workers to claim a building tile and associated VP value, as well as triggering an extra action for first-time builds for each of the six building types. Interestingly, subsequent workers must be placed orthogonally, much like the placement of tokens in the districts in [thing=531][/thing]; as such there is area control and blocking.

Each season, a "Voice of the People" tile is revealed, much like the "Voice of the People" in [thing=554][/thing], where the players must collect specific bonus tiles (from the forum or from military actions) to offer as tribute to placate the people--in terms of "bread" and "circuses" or piety--lest they lose VP. This punitive feature is similar to the "rat track" in Notre Dame, but as with ND, there usually is enough time to prepare. The main focus in satisfying this demand is either through military action by moving a leader into a space with a bonus tile, or initiating the forum action, where there is a random assortment of bonus tiles and bonus action tiles. With other Feld titles, VPs and other incentives are offered to the players briefly, before being removed from the game. The same holds true for the forum tiles.

The senate action has a two-fold function, both determining the seniority order for the next year and correspondingly increasing VP for every subsequent action, but also for determining who will be "consul" and selecting one of two end-of-game bonus conditions. A "vote" is taken at the end of every year, with the tally being the sum of the senator's standing, plus any "vote" bonus tiles they've picked up from forum and military actions. These are similar to the civilization cards in [thing=34635][/thing], since they give more VP for collecting specific commodity cards, or other lucrative VP bonuses.

The shipping action allows players to draw commodity cards randomly, where they try to form unique sets, and them playing them out on their play area. The commodity symbols behave much like the symbols in [thing=9216][/thing], where huge VP counts can be gained for such as 2/3/4 unique commodities, 2/3/4 of of the same, or pairs of commodities. The selected bonus conditions could give additional VP for playing specific commodities.

The last of the six actions is selecting a "Trajan" tile. Surrounding the mancala are six slots, where the Trajan tiles are placed. The last pebble sowed triggers both the action and possible selection of the trajan tile associated with that action. Each Trajan tile has two pebble colors; if the bowl contains the two pebble colors that correspond to that tile, the VP on the tile are awarded, along with an extra build placement, military placement, bread/circus/piety capability, +2 extra action tile, etc. 

As can be seen with this long-winded comment, this seems to be an extremely complex game, with many, many intertwining mechanisms, but each of the six "mini games" is not that difficult. Still, rules explanation took approximately 45 minutes, but familiarity with CoB and other Feld games was helpful in shortening that time. Thus, this is not a game one could conceivably take to a board game meetup, since it would take too much time to set up and explain. The game has strong random elements in how the VP landscape and in how the "Voice of the People" are formed, how the forum bonus tiles fall out, end-of-game bonus conditions, as well as how the Trajan tiles come out. Beyond that, the mancala gives the players complete control over their destinies. So, this is more in line with traditional VP layout games. Still, the players are overwhelmed by the assortment of VP paths available. AP with the mancala is a strong possibility, and with three or four player, downtime could suffer. Despite this, the mancala mechanism alone is a fascinating game mechanism to explore; however, it's internal workings seem to have no corresponding meaning to reality! While the selected actions are meaningful within the context of the theme of ancient Rome, I cannot rationalize [i]why[/i] or [i]how[/i] that action was generated--yeah, the last "pebble" was thrown into the "forum" action bowl, and the colors of two of the pebbles in that bowl happen to match the Trajan tile that is associated with that bowl. What does that mean? Why do I care?  What does fulfilling a Trajan tile really mean? Perhaps that is why some call the theme pasted-on. If one is willing to look past this inherent "weakness" or "disconnect", this is a great game--this may be Feld's best game, but that remains to be seen. Initial rating: "9", with potential to move up.

RATING: 8.25
拿取額外行動容易忘記 由起始玩家開始，從抽牌堆和棄牌堆選取3張商品卡，若棄牌堆空了立刻翻開抽牌堆最上面那張補上。選取完後換下一位玩家選取。
         ◎遊戲結束記分
▲當最後一個季節板塊被移除後遊戲結束開始計分：
△每張在玩家手牌的商品卡1分(非在個人遊戲區的商品卡)
△在工地的工人每個1分
△在軍營的軍人每個1分
△在行動環的圖拉真板塊每個1分
△每3個相同的建築板塊10分(可使用論壇板塊中的萬用建築板塊)
△每4個相同的建築板塊20分(可使用論壇板塊中的萬用建築板塊)
△每個加分板塊的加分
軍事行動:
將軍人移動到軍事領袖所到的省分，若該省分沒有其他的軍人，玩家馬上得到該省分的分數，若有其他軍人，則每有1位軍人少得3分。        玩家從下面4個動作擇一執行
海港行動:
1.從抽牌堆抽取2張商品卡至手牌，然後從手牌中選擇一張商品卡，面向上放到2個棄牌堆其中一堆上。
2.從兩個棄牌堆擇一拿取最上方的那張商品卡。
3.玩家出1張或2張商品卡到個人遊戲區，並從抽牌堆抽回與出牌數量相等的商品卡，個人遊戲區中的商品卡，遊戲結束時用來滿足加分板塊的條件。
4.玩家出一組符合商船條件的商品卡，並獲得對應的分數，得分後將商品卡置於個人遊戲區(遊戲結束時可用於加分板塊的加分)。得分後若商船是彩面向上，將之翻到灰面；若原本就是灰面，則維持原狀。

RATING: 4
This game has a lot going for it. Great theme, fascinating point structure, and it all centers around a mancala-like bowl in which you determine your order of actions as well as earn bonus abilities. 

There are several separate play areas on the board, and like Shadows Over Camelot this creates heavy pressure of having to do several things in several areas while limited to only one move per turn unless you manage to chain actions. I thought the Mancala like mat that every player gets was a highly fascinating mechanism, which I would try to plan several turns in advance on. The time-lapse device was also pretty ingenious. In the end though, Trajan felt kind of mechanical. While the game was a mildly fun variant on the worker placement genre, none of the separate areas really felt compellingly immersive. Nothing about the game really enthralled me or caused me to want to play again. That said, I can understand why it might be appealing to some as a strategy game with low player interaction or conflict.

RATING: 8.5
Exceptional Euro style game. Trajan has several legitimate paths to victory.  Each turn is short as each player only makes one brief action.  This game has a relatively mild degree of player interaction, however opponents will frequently take tiles from the board or perform actions that foil well laid plans.  You can make things more difficult for your opponents but you can't totally destroy them.  The mancala mechanic implementation as a means to choose your action totally rocks.  I feel like this is the perfect game.  You must plan a few moves ahead in advance and it is very stimulating to analyze the game in progress and plotting how to maximize the victory point yield of your actions.  This game shares a trait with Castles of Burgandy, to wit: clever combo actions can be carried out, with proper planning, that are super productive.  Successful completion of a big combo move is a compelling rush.  Play Trajan; it rocks!

RATING: 3
I don't like most Stefan Feld games. Trajan, as with Castles of Burgundy, has several different mini-games intertwined, with multiple paths to victory that translate in practice to (1) the need to specialize and (2) relatively little player interaction, and it's pretty easy for oversights to occur in terms of keeping track of everything on the board and all the different scorings that happen. I didn't care the for the mancala element; I'm not crazy about mancala in the first place, and here it just felt like a rondel on which you play a separate mini-game.

------------------------

Alternative version for those who prefer more snark: 
--In Trajan you are playing mancala . . . and a few other similarly abstract games at the same time. 
--Well, why don't we just break out the GIPF series and do that thing with the potentials where you can play the games all together? 
--Because it would be really thinky and we don't like abstracts that much. But now that I think of it, Trajan is pretty abstract and thinky, plus the rules are way more fiddly, such that the rules explanation will take much longer and during the game we're really likely to make some small error that will end up deciding the winner -- but it's got a pasted on Roman theme!
--Oh. Yay?

RATING: 7.5
Se trata de un juego en la línea de su autor Stefan Feld. Euro con el tema totalmente pegado pero que es agradable de jugar. Un tablero central donde vamos haciendo diferentes acciones que simbolizan la conquista de un territorio, la construcción de una ciudad, el mercadeo con productos de la época, entre otros, 

Como otros juegos de este hombre hay una mecánica central, "idea feliz", que en este caso es el uso de una especie de Mancala para la selección de la acción a realizar por el jugador. Cada jugador tiene un tablerito personal con seis cuencos que tienen unos cilindros de colorines. Cuando le toca jugar elige un cuenco, coge los cilindros del mismo y va colocando en sentido horario uno de esos cilindros en el resto de cuencos. Hará la acción asociada al cuenco donde coloque el último cilindro. Además, los cilindros tienen colorines porque asociados a los cuencos podemos tener unas losetas con beneficios que se activan si tenemos los cilindros de colores adecuados en el cuenco activado. Vamos que puede resultar un poco propenso al AP si hay alguien que se dedica a pensar en un plazo muy largo.

Si no hay nadie muy tontorrón con el tema de planificar se puede jugar en un par de horitas como parte de una sesión de tarde

RATING: 8.3
In Trajan, players are Roman nobility during the pinnacle of the Roman Empire seeking to further develop Rome and extend its reach through constructing public works, military deployment, political influence and mercantilism.   The player who best navigates the intricacies of the central game mechanic – the mancala – by timely stringing together actions to earn the most victory points wins the game.  

Thus far, this is my favorite design from Stefan Feld.  The innovative mancala mechanism creates tremendous opportunity for tactical and strategic planning.  

Weighted Rating: 8.3 (Fun 8; Gameplay 10; Components 8; Theme 5)
Formula:
[Weighted rating = (((Fun*4) + (Gameplay*3) + (Components *2) + (Theme *1))/10)]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Really great game. It seems all Feld games are some twist on actin selection, usually with some sort of restriction to which actions you can select. In this one the restriction is a personal mancala mechanism, so proper planning means you're never locked out of the actions you want. The game itself is made up of a bunch of minigames, but the genius of the game is how interconnected they all end up being, so the game does feel like a cohesive whole. And the way the Trajan tiles work is amazing as well, they force you to work harder planing your mancala more precisely in exchange for some really powerful bonuses.

RATING: 5.5
2013-06-14

Initial Rating: 8.0 (October 2012)

I’m now officially a Feld fan. This game is very interesting, from the Mancala mechanic to (try to) plan ahead your moves as best as you can, to the demands at the end of each year. Particularly the demand mechanic is great as it creates a tense game (a la Notre Dame). I found the game to have a lot of interesting decisions, it is not a heavy game, but planning in advance of two turns can be very challenging. 

On the bad side, the theme is (sadly) paper thin, I didn’t felt it at all in my plays. Also, I’m worried about the replayability factor, perhaps it is best to play this from time to time as each game plays very similar. The Mancala mechanic (although great) can be very repetitive. 

I’m downgrading it to a 7.0 because I no longer want to play this every time. Still I consider it to be a good game that I would like to play from time to time. When I first played Trajan, I thought it was the best Feld game, however, nowadays, I’m longing to play more Castles of Burgundy. 

Current Rating 7.0 
[hr]
2014-03-17

As I´m writing this, I have logged 13 plays. My initial fear became true. For me, the game sorely lacks in the replayability department. Nowadays, I almost always oppose a game of Trajan, the game plays too samey for me each time. I recognize it is a novel design but Trajan has fall out of favor. Now it is one of my least favorite Felds. I´m glad I sold my copy.

RATING: N/A
[b][url=https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/864240/2012-golden-geek-awards-nominees-announced-voting]2012 Golden Geek Nominee[/url][/b]

Set in ancient Rome, Trajan is a development game in which players try to increase their influence and power in various areas of Roman life such as political influence, trading, military dominion and other important parts of Roman culture. The central mechanism of the game uses a system similar to that in Mancala or pit-and-pebbles games. In Trajan, a player has six possible actions: building, trading, taking tiles from the forum, using the military, influencing the Senate and placing Trajan tiles on his tableau. This is a Euro-style strategy game and playing time is 30 minutes per player.

Players: 2, 3, 4
Time: 90 min
Mechanics: [property=2046][/property], [property=2041][/property], [property=2040][/property], [property=2004][/property]

RATING: 8
I will say I have yet to play a badly designed Feld game, but neither have any of his games been home runs or personal favorites. For me, his games run the spectrum of meh to really enjoyable and Trajan is definitely in the latter camp.

The core of Trajan is pure mancala-inspired action mechanic where a player picks up a pile of colored action stones from one bowl and distributes them, one at a time, to other bowls in a clockwise direction. The last bowl to get an action stone is the action that the player performs. There are lot of tiles to collect, people you can turn into workers or legionnaires, senate voting for end game points, people's demands to satisfy, bonus actions to gain,... and more! Most of these choices generate points (or prevent from losing them), hence the "point salad" label. Despite the amount of small point increments players can constantly gain, there are specific paths that generate larger point totals over a few turns and players who plan best balancing short and longer-term opportunities will come out ahead. I very much enjoyed manipulating the mancala stones to get the actions and bonus actions I was shooting for and figuring out where to most effectively expend my energy. Really good stuff!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
After one play with 2, a great Euro design.   Want to play with 3 or 4 soon to solidify my thoughts on it, but so far, it's a keeper. Lots to do and not enough time to do it.

RATING: 9.2
There's so many different things going on in this game, and it's a testament to the design that things still go along snappily and I never had to really sit and think and calculate as I have to do in other games that have a similar level of complexity (e.g. Confucius, Dominant Species or Power Grid).  It feels almost like a "euro olympics" because there are kind of 6 minigames going on at the same time, and, while they are inter-related, and while the Mancala is the "gear-wheel" for all of them, it still doesn't feel as cohesive as Feld's other games.  Not that that's a bad thing, just an observation.  Now that I think about it, this aspect of Trajan is actually nicely thematically appropriate given the "epic" scope of the subject matter, vs. the more intimate Luna or Strasbourg for example. (And, yes, I am one who enjoys the themes of euros rather than ignoring them :D) The Mancala elevates the game to a very unusual and interesting experience.

After 4 plays, upgraded from 8.5 to 9.2.  One of Felds' best.

RATING: 8
Trajan was the heavy Euro game that interested me the most at Spiel 2011. Trajan didn't disappoint. Lots of options, an innovative central mechanism inspired by Mancala and potentially many ways to winning. My only concern is that there won't be much differentiation among equally experienced players as any selected strategy will yield a comparable amount of VP's and small efficiency advantages which won't even be noticed at the time could decide the game. Nonetheless, I almost bought the game. I only decided otherwise, because I found Feld's last year release Luna for half of the money they were asking for Trajan which shares the same characteristic of many ways to generate VPs.

RATING: 7
Trajan is a solid Euro worker placement game with a mancala-style action selection mechanism. The use of the pieces in the wheel limits your choices, but does so in a way that really forces you to plan ahead. You can set yourself up for huge combos if you plan well, as it's possible to place multiple meeples and take multiple actions in a single turn. The theme, well... it's pretty much irrelevant. But the board, pieces and rulebook are all top-notch. This one definitely suffers from analysis paralysis, but there is also no downtime, as you are constantly analyzing your own possibilities. Suffers from the problem that it is a game of calculation, so if you don't feel like calculating constantly-shifting possibilities you won't feel like playing it.

RATING: 10
[b]Trajan[/b] is typical Feld's point salad game. Its like 5-6 little games put into one package, that are not tightly interconnected (if at all). 
Theme is pasted on completely, but this one somehow fits perfectly. 
Main mechanic (which is typical for Feld to have one central thing in his games) is Mancala, while there is also set collection, area movement, hand management,etc. 
Whole game you'll feel like trying to solve different puzzles and as game is really good optimized, you'll never feel left behind with nothing to do. It's always some other option. It's hard to describe it as it is abstract in nature (with pasted-on theme).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Another keeper from Feld who is quickly becoming one of my favorite designers - his sheer ability to blend different gaming elements and mechanics into something that is enjoyable is quite impressive.  Complete lack of theme integration is not a big deal for me (which this game significantly suffers from). For the most part, the game is a fairly solitaire experience but does have a few actions that do benefit the players who do them earlier so there is a bit of a race element. Using the Mancala mechanic in an effective way to set up future desired actions takes some getting used to if you are not familiar with it. In terms of scoring options, there are 6 primary actions, some of which have several sub options and can also trigger the Trajan tiles – all of which reminded me of Feld’s Castle of Burgundy (in terms of ways to score points). The variable numbers of turns each round did not seem to be a big factor in our games so far, but I could see players using that more effectively to end a round prematurely if the situation presents itself. Getting the 2X markers and the permanent demand tiles early in the game can free up a lot of other actions as well as create some nice combos later in the game. Game length for 4 new players was around 2.5 hours, but I can see that dropping to 30m/player after a few games which is similar to Luna and Burgundy. So far, 4 players appears to be the best player count due do the increased competition for the different areas.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Felds gone hardcore.  His signature is there, with population needs that have to be fulfilled every quarter to avoid penalty.  The core mechanic is Mancala, the ancestral African game plundered very effectively.  Around this, multiple independent minigames allow the player to rack up points based on the output of the Mancala mechanic.  Trajan is a very dense and rich game with a an exceptional number of paths to victory that involves a high amount of thinking.  The theme is there but not particularly strong.  Medium player interaction, mostly competitive, but very controlled and little/no ensuing chaos/randomness.  The Felds game of choice for the cerebral crowd.

RATING: 6
Played at Unity Games XVIII. This one was white-hot at Unity and was on my list to try out, even though I haven't been that crazy about Stefan Feld's games. Feld tends to come up with one interesting mechanic and then sticks it into a bland, by-the-numbers exercise. Trajan has the requisite weirdo bit (a mancala board to determine actions) and then throws just about everything else into the mix. It's hard to NOT get some points on every turn -- hell, at one point I sneezed and got four points for it. It was a good enough time, and I'd play again, but it did kind of feel like someone took the "lost bits" box from last year's Unity and made a game with them. (To be fair, I did terrible, and the guy who played before did well, so there is a thing going on here.)

RATING: 10
This game is great.  It can be as random or strategic as you make it based on how you manipulate your own board.  Once you've got that down, you need to contend with other players getting in your way going after points.  It does a great job of balancing points you collect along the way with end game points.  Also, it's nearly impossible to win by dominating one area of the board, you need to diversify (among the senate, military, shipping, and workers) during the game which makes it very challenging and very fun.

RATING: 6
After five plays of [b][thing=102680][/thing][/b], it appeared to be holding its own as a two-player game to play against my wife.  She enjoyed the mancala puzzle and so did I.  But after a few more plays, we both kind of got bored of even the mancala and the accompanying smorgasbord of VP-generating actions.  In spite of the smorgasbord, it appears that concentration in the Construction area is key to winning the two-player game.  In all five games we've played, whomever has had the most points generated from this area has won the game.  I hope that at some point I might be proven wrong on this hypothesis.  The biggest criticism I have of the game in the awkward turn mechanic.  As simple as it is (advance X on the mancala, advance X on the turn track), it's not intuitive to actually move the turn marker.  I'm positive our games have run longer due to one of us being so involved in our move and the subsequent actions, that we've forgotten to advance the turn marker.

RATING: 8
I love Stefan Feld designs, and this game is no exception. The main game mechanism, the mancala, is a great puzzle. Player interaction is fairly indirect, although since it's often obvious what others are planning for, there is quite a bit of effort spent on figuring out whether or not you can take a certain action before someone else beats you to it.

The absence of luck in this game means it requires more mental effort than some of the Alea-published Feld designs, which makes this a weightier game. Consequently, we don't play it as often as we do Notre Dame, MAcao, and Castles of Burgundy, but it is nevertheless a fantastic game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I must admit, this game is dry. I am a Stefan Feld fan and I think Trajan is the less appealing (in terms of "charming power") of the games I've tried so far from this designer; neverthless, this game provedes a huge bunch of fun! You challenge yourself before your opponents so every time you try to push your skills further, because at the end of each game, any time, you'll have the feeling that something cuold have been done better.
Moreover, I've always liked how in Feld's games the mechanics merge together like a perfect gear and this aspect really shines in Trajan. Not to mention the Mancala wheel, which is really tremendous and makes the turns absolutely not repetitive (which, instead, is something I noticed in other Feld games.

RATING: 8
I hadn't played this for quite a while and had forgotton how great it is. I guess this is up there with Bora Bora for my favourite feld game (these will probably be joined by Castles of Burgundy if I ever get around to playing it again).

Brilliant game that I can understand isn't for everybody. 6 sub games on the main board are linked by a brilliant mancala-action-selection-gonna-fuck-up-your-plans-and-laugh-in-your-face type thing on your individual player boards.

It's a head scratcher, but one that I really enjoy and would love to play any time. 

Oh did I mention it is set in the roman empire and you are competing to head the senate, use the forum, build buildings, gain military conquests, trade goods and expand the roman empire? Could almost be a Knizia!

RATING: 9
What an awesome game. 

Update 12/31/14: I've considered trading this game several times, not because I don't like it, but because it's not one of Erin's favorites. To be fair, it is quite long, and I can see how it could be dry for some. What makes this game for me though is its puzzle like feel. I love the mancala. It's such a great implementation! Personally, I quite like the theme, being a world history teacher (crazy bias, of course). I also love the component quality. Kudos to HUCH Games for making some of the thickest cardboard playing boards I've ever seen in a game. If Castles of Burgandy had components like Trajan, I would pick it up in a second. I'm going to fight to hold onto this game. It's good, really good! Turns are quick and games are not overly long, especially at 2 player level. My biggest complaint is the setup time, which can be a good 15-20 minutes, if I'm doing it alone. This is by far my favorite Feld game. I like a lot of his games, but I think I love this one. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I can respect the unique roundel mechanic, as well as the many different scoring opportunities of trajan. However, its not my favorite Feld. I found the roundel to be slightly analysis paralysis inducing. It's hard to plan more than two turns in advance, which relegates my next turns to being whatever the leftovers are; and it may take a couple turns to set anything back up. That's the catch 22 of the mechanic, but it can be frustrating.. Also, i found the theme to be ok, it wasn't as pasted on as some say. What hurt it more was the abstract nature that some of the scoring options were presented as. That's the part i didn't care for. But it's a good game, and scoring points pretty much every turn makes me feel all warm inside.

RATING: 6
I can see why people like this one, but it wasn't a good fit for me.

What I like:
- Multiple paths to victory through specialization
- Mancala puts you in control of your own destiny
- No direct conflict
- Variable round length

What I don't like:
- Too much of a brain burner for me and my gaming group
- Lack of theme did not keep us engaged throughout the game. Each mini game felt independent of each other and it did not really feel like playing one complete game.
- It's hard to visualize your progress (besides points) throughout the game, compared to other games we like where you can see your board/engine building up as the game goes on.

RATING: 8.5
Solid point salad game with various routes to victory.  Mancala mechanic adds an interesting twist compared to more common action selection mechanics.  2p game has some direct competition for prime action spots on the board--enough to sometimes force a rethink of long term strategy.  Battles for senate supremacy can get particularly heated with desirable bonus tiles at stake.  Conflict is likely much better with more players though.

The only luck comes from random order of tiles and cards, but this is largely offset by planning ahead with the mancala and proper timing of actions.  No rubber banding mechanic either, so the game doesn't prevent players from falling behind.  Then again there's no engine building so a runaway leader only happens if the other players play poorly.

Very rewarding and satisfying game.

RATING: 8
Feels like a combo of Castles of Burgundy (with the possibility of chain moves) and Bora Bora (with the series of mini games). Less fiddly than Bora Bora by far; no little rules to remember and much less maintenance between phases/quarters.

The lack of dice is somewhat refreshing. Though I do enjoy mitigating the luck of dice (Macao/CoB/Bora), being able to plan many moves ahead on the mancala is a nice change of pace. The game is still tactical in many ways, but there is the possibility for development of longer term strategies as well.

With two players, value of Senate is decreased and value of construction is huge due to less competition, making it easier to get sets of 3/4. I expect the sweet spot to be three players, balancing competition in each mini game while not introducing too much downtime.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
02/11/12 Played a three-player game.
- There is no direct player interaction. The worst you can do is take a tile you think someone else might want. You can't even block opponents in the builder or military areas. Its pretty much multi-player solitaire. 
- There is a *lot* going on. Its difficult to keep all the balls in the air as you juggle what to do and in what order. One could say there are too many ways to score points. All this leads to analysis paralysis / brain burning. 
- The mancala mechanic is fresh, but it and the unnamed action cylinders bear no resemblance to reality. 
- The theme is very thin. 
- Trajan seems more puzzle than game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Stefan Feld VP salad game. There is a serious disconnect between the theme and mechanisms, but that doesn't bother me. Every damn thing you do gets you a point. Seriously. Again, it doesn't bother me but I can understand those who ARE bothered.

There are 6 different types of actions in the game, and there are many short-term and long-term ways to score points. These actions are chosen by moving stones around a mancala - sometimes you'll be unable to take the action you need, or you'll have to plan ahead and move your stones accordingly to ensure you get the action you need. There are also bonuses for using certain colors of stones to trigger certain actions. It's a handful and 100% an efficiency exercise. Just from reading this description, you'll know whether you'll like this or not.

I like this. My wife does too, and it takes us about 75 minutes to finish a game. I feel like adding more players only really adds more downtime, and it seems like 2 is best (though I haven't tried 3, the 4 player game had some downtime issues).

Gave this to Bryan.

RATING: 10
When you pick up the box you know instantly that this game means business. It is heavy as a brick and includes a ton of thick cardboard and beautiful wooden pieces. 

Set up can be a time consuming task because there are som many moving parts. Zip lock bags and little extra work in organizing when putting game pieces in box pay off when you plan to play Trajan again. 

Game has so many possibilities and so little time to do what you plan to do. Every sub-game in this game is a race where most points go to that player who does things first. 

Usually my heart skips beats and throat is dry when I am  constanly trying to find my wyliest move and at the same time plan few moves ahead. And while I am trying to get all the planets aligned time my counter players are doing what they can to beat me in my own goals. 

Mancala mechanic as an action selection tool is heat and soul of this game and is executed brilliantly. 

This game is perfect, for me at least.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
This is a "nose down" game from start to finish, so I'm never going to be wild about it. 

That said, I liked it considerably more than any other nose-down Feld game I've played.

EDIT: Thinking about my first play some more [I guess that in itself is a good-ish sign], I think the fatal flaw here is that a lot of the mechanisms seem to have been added on for [i]no reason[/i] other than to add something to analyze [with no corresponding additional gameplay]. 

There's no reason, for instance, to have the color-matching requirement on the Trajan tiles. It's relatively easy, with sufficient analysis, to get all of your colors to line up in all of the right places at all of the right times. You don't miss activations except for not having analyzed.

There is no gameplay decisions / opportunity cost to be made in choosing how to distribute the colors. 

You can always make sure that everything gets activated. The entire mechanic should have been scrapped.

There should only be non-colored "beads" in the bins. All Trajan tiles should auto-activate when targeted.

Why? Because this is effectively what happens in the game anyway, given sufficient time for analysis.

RATING: 8
After 1 play, I really like this game. It is like playing a whole series of different games in one - which I know some people will not like, as there are so many paths to gaining VPs. If you try to cover all posisble paths you will just end up confused, but pick on one or two particular paths and you can maximise your VP collection.
The Mancala style rondel mechanic is different and effective, providing you dont over analyse all possible moves and plan too far ahead as you will slow down the quite fast pace of the turns.
In summary I think this will be a love/hate Marmite game and for me it is tremendous.

After 3 plays I am becoming less convinced by this game. If played quickly it is tremendous..... But (and this is a very big BUT) if players spend too long analysing their moves them game becomes far too long, so be careful who you ah this with. I have adjusted my rating accordingly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
In this Roman themed game, each player has a "Mancala" board with 6 trays. You seed each tray with 2 cubes. Your turn first consists of moving cubes from one tray to another. Where the last cube ends up, you first check to see if you match the colors of a bonus tile. Afterwards, depending on which tray you landed at, you have one of 6 possible actions to take: adding another bonus tile, going to the forum to get tiles, going to the senate to earn VPs, army movement, construction movement and shipping. THe game is made up of four quarters. Each quarter, the people demand three of religion, bread or gladiator: if you don't meet some of these needs you lose VPs. So you have to consider if you want to meet these needs. 

This is a good game, and there are many strategic paths to choose. For instance, you could try to use a +2 token in the forum, or +2 in shipping, and so forth.You can't do everything, and there is tension on what you can do before your opponent can do it, but you can't be top dog everywhere. This is a good game, with many possibilities.

This game would get my vote for game of the year for 2012.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[b]Mechanics: 5/5[/b] None of the mechanics are special on their own. If you really look at each of the actions as they are, they're really basic and boring.

The mancala isn't original either. BUT, the way these things weave together, along with the Trajan tiles, the bonus actions, and the time track, shoots these individually mediocre mechanisms into something that is nothing short of absolute genius. The mechanics make this game. I honestly think the fact that the individual mechanics are so simple and uninspired makes it even better, because it would take something spectacular to turn these into something fun, thought provoking and interesting. And Stefen Feld achieved it with flying colors.

[b]Art: 3/5[/b] The game really does look pretty drab, but it's thematic. The personal board looks like an ancient roman tablet. The artwork on the tiles and cards is serviceable, but it is nothing to write home about. It's all pretty bland looking, but it doesn't cross the line into bad territory.

[b]Scoring: 4/5[/b] I'm learning lately that point salads aren't my thing, which is ironic considering the fact I purchased a point salad microbadge. It seems like everything that you do gives you points, and it's difficult to conjure up ways to take the best move. Construction seems overpowered 90% of the time, but sometimes it gets setup in a way that keeps it from being too strong. End game scoring is usually pretty annoying to me in most games if it isn't just a single scoring objective like in Troyes, but you have a lot of control of who gets what tiles if you're willing to spend actions getting votes. I have, many a time, blocked my opponents from getting tiles they would obviously want by disregarding all other actions to get votes in the senate either through tiles, or through actually taking the senate action. This contrasts well with drafting end game scoring tiles/cards, because it adds another layer of strategy, forcing you to decide if the end game scoring tiles are worth fighting for, when there are so many other things that can net you a good chunk of points as well.

[b]Luck: 3/5[/b] Overall, this game is really good with keeping luck to a minimum. But the whole mechanic around the shipping actions is somewhat luck based, and that's really awkward in a game that otherwise has near-perfect information and a way to plan from the very beginning how you want to play. 

Also, each turn, when setting up pieces on the provinces, sometimes someone will get a tile that is without a doubt perfect for the situation placed right next to their legionnaire. Usually these are either senate votes or demand tiles for the last round. So, if your legionnaire is on the other side of the map from your opponents, and the demand tiles flips to show that the citizens want bread, and the only bread on the entire board after the forum and provinces are filled is next to your opponent... yeah... it sucks when people get advantages like that especially in games like this that almost seem like peak potential games in terms of outplay potential.

In most games, this amount of luck would probably only drop it to a 4/5 rating, but because of how much planning and strategy go into the remainder of this game, these problems actually hurt the game more than they would many others.

[b]Fiddliness: 3/5 [/b] This dropped the games rating from a 9 to an 8 on a recent play. I wish people could keep track of the time track on their own turn, but that never happens. You have one person who will be keeping track of the time marker, but that means they can never get even a little distracted. Every turn, having to refill the forum can also kill a bit of the flow. Otherwise, it's not too fiddly, but these things are kind of a problem.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Typically Feldian euro. The mancala is really interesting and thought-provoking and is basically the meat of the game. The Trajan tiles which also make you consider mancala colours are a nice touch and keep you on your toes.

On the other hand, it disturbs me that there is basically no relation whatsoever between the six "mini-games". I don't mind a good point salad, but I prefer there to be some interconnection between options - so that they improve each other - rather than just a bunch of separate things. It's sometimes said that "there's a lot going on, but it hangs together well". In this case, there's a lot going on, and it doesn't hang together at all!

The theme's also pasted on, but that's not a big deal for me.

Ultimately, it seems that the underlying current to Feld's games is that they are one innovative and interesting element mixed with a bunch of standard stuff, which saddens me somewhat.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Feels like you are putting an expensive race-car to the test. So many interconnected mechanisms, and all of them make sense in the context of all the others. The way the punishments works makes sense -- the demand of the Roman populace -- and that demand drives the game. However, your open victory point chits can make you zig away from meeting the demand, if you can work toward them and make more points than you lose due to demand.

The mancala = what action you take is brilliant. Can back you into a corner, so plan accordingly.

Love the way you need to concentrate on 2 of the 6 areas to win. Specializing in 1 is a good way to lose, but in style. Generalizing in 5+ areas is a certain path to destruction, as you never seem to get the good stuff in specific areas; however, you can still muck up others' plans by taking a tactical move in one of those places you do not plan on devoting a lot of time on. This is a game about picking the time & place of your battles, which is quite appealing.

RATING: 7.5
A swathe of mini games all tied together with a mancala action selection device. Wrangling with the mancala device is the best part of the game – get that part tied up and you’ll have a good chance at scoring points from mini games – sorry, I mean scoring lots points from the mini games, as virtually everything you do will score you points – whether it’s from the point track, the point map, the point tiles, the other point tiles or from the point cards. Playing this game feels like your shovelling coal to power a steam engine – you need to keep shovelling to keep moving along the point track. Stop shovelling and you’ll coast along, still picking up points, but you’ll fall behind and won’t have a big enough head of steam to race to victory at the end. 

I think it has a Roman theme...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Boasting a plethora of material and not as much innovation in mechanics by themselves as in the combination of known procedures, Trajan is an epitomic German board game: players aim for success (scoring) balancing military campaigns, gathering influence at the Senate, keeping commerce lively, building, selecting bonus actions to achieve and browsing for the right opportunity at the forum. Each of these actions is like a little drafting game on its own; all of them are linked by a Mancala-style wheel to determine the next mini-game to play and trigger extra actions and by common tiles appearing on several different mini-games. The game is structured in four trimesters; at the end of each players must have gathered several special chips (in the sense of feeding) to avoid penalties.

Even though an actual move is just choosing one pit in the wheel, sowing and carrying out the respective action(s), the six mini-games have their own intricacies, choosing which one to play isn't trivial at all since there are many paths to the win and being able to play the wished sequence of mini-games at all (let alone trigger extra actions) demands extremely judicious use of the wheel. Even though a new player can easily understand how to move, grasping the relationship between all the layers in the game takes repeated playing. 

Trajan is a complex, rotund game, with just indirect interaction to keep a fine balance between strategy and tactics. For players of abstract games or family games it will feel like too much of a chunk. For enthusiasts of Eurogames, it will be a joy to play eight games at once.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Il gioco gira intorno al meccanismo del mancala. Stefan Feld, che non si è mai distinto per giochi ben ambientati, propone un giocone con meccaniche riuscite e ambientazione nulla. Insieme a Bora Bora e In the year ofthe Dragon, Trajan è al momento il suo gioco più complesso e permette ai giocatori di scegliere ad ogni turno tra sei differenti azioni. L'originalità del titolo sta proprio nel fatto che le azioni scelte possono essere eseguite solo se l'ultimo cubetto del mancala termina sopra il movimento prescelto. In virtù di questo riuscito meccanismo Trajan offre una buona interazione e la necessita al tempo stesso di fare un'attenta programmazione. Durante la partita si avrà la tendenza a specializzarsi in qualche settore del tabellone: battaglia, commercio, senato, manodopera. Non ci sono particolari tempi morti (salvo pensatori troppo fini) ma spesso si rischia di concentrarsi solo sul proprio mancala perdendo di vista l'andamento delle plance degli altri giocatori. Personalmente trovo molto bella la prospettiva del tabellone che mostra una visione dell'Europa partendo dal Senato di Roma.

RATING: 7.5
Abstract Euro with a pasted-on theme.  Doesn't mean that its bad or anything, but don't expect some kind of "Roman" or "political" game out of this.

I like the various paths to scoring, the constant worry about the senate vote, the pressure to take actions to score vs. actions to prevent losing points and the never enough time to do everything you want.  I like that it takes time to understand basic strategies/tactics.

Most people I have played with seem to like it, so I wont be getting rid of it.  Personally, I don't really enjoy it - maybe because its got such a mess of things going on?  Maybe its because there is virtually no engine to construct?  Not 100% sure why, but it does feel like a bit of a mess of a game.  I come to these feelings after 9 plays of this game.

Sold via auction 4/4/15.

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]I managed to get a second play in at TotalCon this year and loved it. I have played a lot of Feld's and this is one of my favorites (CoB being the other). Of the games I've played that riff on the mancala I like how Trajan does it best (I've also played 5 Tribes and Staufer Dynasty). 

I love the way I am forced to think in this game; there is a spacial puzzle to the mancala wheel that you really need to wrap your mind around and it hits a sweet spot for me.

The only reason Trajan's not in my collection is that this game not only demands a lot of forward planning, you also have to manipulate the mancala in order to be able to select the actions you want (usually for multiple turns in a row). Trajan is too much of a brain burner to be enjoyed by my wife and kids, but I'm hopeful that I'll get to play more since one of my gaming buddies loves Trajan.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (n/a):[/COLOR]

[i]I really enjoyed this game, but unfortunately I can not imagine anyone in my family wanting to play this. The multiple ways to score points in conjunction with managing your mancala would probably even overwhelm my son. The guys I played with played at a brisk pace, but I can see this game inducing AP in lots of people. I would love to play Trajan again.[/i]

RATING: 9
心得:
行動概念是撥棋
兩人遊戲比較難只專走某一路線
規則:
1.起始玩家在議會的最下面
2.彩色棋要再放圖拉真版塊前擺好
3.抽牌堆旁各一個棄牌推，由起始玩家開始抽三張牌，如果抽棄牌推馬上補一張頂牌在棄牌堆
4.每個人照起始順序選3個圖拉真版塊放在246，不能重複，拱門放在1
只有移動棋是必須行動，其它行動不強迫執行。
5.海港行動:四選一 A暗抽兩張從手上棄一張 B抽棄牌一張 C出1~2張牌，並直接補牌庫頂兩張 D出貨卡到船上，並把船翻過來
6.軍事行動: 如果選擇派兵去將軍拿就拿地圖上的分數，如果有其他人在就少三分，一塊地圖只能有一個自己的兵
7.圖拉真行動: 沒空格的話不能執行
8.建築行動: 可以採別人已經在的位置
9.補充時地圖沒人得才補，船轉回來
10.最後記分:沒出的卡，工人營或兵營的人，未完成的圖拉真版塊都一分
三個一樣建築10分，四個20分
11.增加行動一次只能用一塊
12.綠色一個九分版塊最高只能拿九分

RATING: 6.5
The most laborious and unnecessarily complex of Feld's games. As always with his games, Feld has a collection of victory point salad mechanics with tiles, tokens, and cards with absolutely no relationship to its theme. And, as always, there just isn't enough time or turns to do what you want! Which is actually kind of genius. Anyways, Trajan's central mechanic is mancala or a version of it; the last space you dropped a token in is the action you take which can be maddening because, for a couple of turns, it's possible that you can't take the action you need because your tokens aren't arranged that way. It would be pointless! Fruitless! Brainless to even suggest a long-term strategy for this game; you would go cross-eyed with the attempt. The trick is to strategize a move or two ahead and you'll end up better than most. For my taste, I would prefer Bora Bora or Castles of Burgundy. Save Trajan for those who care about the tortures of mancala.

RATING: 8
It's a bit of a beast to learn how to tame and do well in. Which of course is majorly attractive, and different enough from other games out there that it has ongoing replay appeal solely for that reason. The trick it seems is specialising in a few of the 6 actions, but the game makes it challenging to achieve that through the clever use of its mancala mechanic. Each action score points in a different way, mainly via different set collections. The trick is working out how to manage your Mancala board so as to repeatedly hit the actions that you're wanting to specialise in so as to earn the bonuses for major set collection prowess. There's also the constant allure of earning bonus Trajan tiles, which you earn while you're doing other stuff by managing to get your Mancala cubes spread about the board in particular ways. All of which induces pleasant agony for some, but you can easily see how it would be considered mindblowingly dull analysis paralysis in others. A game that's not for the faint-hearted, but offers rich reward for those who enjoy managing the option maze.

RATING: 7
A lot going on. Perhaps Feld's heaviest Euro. A collage of mechanisms that work together to produce quite a game. The Mancala-style action-selection mechanism is used in place of dice and worker placement, and it works well; please understand that Trajan is NOT "Mancala: The Board Game".

As as beautiful and well-designed this is, the theme/story doesn't come out enough for me, making this feel more mechanically abstract than fun.

2 player works very well.

RATING: 6.5

So the game really is optimizing a spreadsheet.  There are games I enjoy that feature the same thing, but to me Trajan is so disjointed that I never feel like I am doing anything more than a spreadsheet.  I will play it again at some point, but it will never be a game I seek to play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
With Trajan, Feld gives us another interesting new mechanic to enjoy: a mancala-like action-selection wheel that seems like a cross between Finca and Goa.  The game features a number of diverse point-gathering strategies (some of which work together better than others), allowing players to specialize to a degree.  You don't really have full control of what actions you take, so you end of doing some of everything, though.  What really makes the game interesting is the Trajan tiles, which stipulate that if you get certain combination of specific action markers (which are colored only for this purpose) in certain spots, you'll get big bonuses, like free actions or lots of points.  This aspect of the game encourages you to ignore which actions you're taking, and instead plan ahead two or three turns in advance to try to scoop up those Trajan bonuses.  Of course, it's best if you can make your action strategy and your Trajan strategy line up, but that's quite a feat.

(5+ plays)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Very good first impression and it only got better with repeated play.

Players are faced with a typical Feldian dilemma: there's a lot of stuff to do but, at the same time, their actions are constrained by some strange and opaque mechanic (in this case, the mancala-rondel, or whatever people call it these days). 

But Trajan's central mechanic is truly inspired and it makes the game work. Not only managing the mancala is a fun game in itself, but the clever ways in which it affects the other game elements (the timer, the tiles, etc) are very well thought out.  

Trajan is a very unique and interesting game and it may well be Stefan Feld's masterpiece.

RATING: 9
This game is crazy! This is by far the heaviest Feld I have tried. The sandboxy nature of this game is the reason why you will be scratching/nodding/shaking your head multiple times during the game. There are only 6 actions in this game but a few sub-actions under each one. Not to mention that these actions are activated only when you place a marker on the specific space of the rondel. So no marker on rondel = no activation for that action. Careful planning of your marker placements are essential to pull off some amazing combos. Not to mention that the game does not have a set specific amount of turns you will have as this is determined by the players' actions. 
The reviews are right about this. It is really a multiplayer solitaire and a lot of people do not like it due to the sandbox nature of it. A 4p game is better as it mitigates the sandbox nature, limiting the actions you can optimally perform. This reminds me of that tight gameplay feeling evident in Bora Bora. My first 2p game lasted two hours and a 4p game finished in about the same time. The board does not change with lower player counts except for the forum section and time tracker so at 2p, I feel the board is really open and causes players to be AP prone. Still fun with the different player counts. This game is all about manipulating the rondel on your player board and it is so much fun when you see the action on your player board translate into the main board followed by some "Damn you! I was going to do that next!". 
Trajan is solitaire done right. I foresee this being a favourite of mine.

17/9 - No longer a favourite. Bora Bora in terms of Felds.

RATING: 8
This game is so busy and somewhat action-salad-esque that it should drive me nuts in the same way that I found Dungeon Petz & LeHavre too busy.

Sure, the set cards and tile placements can randomly benefit one player or the other from time to time, but manipulating the mancala allows a player to find a path that matches their strategy and think as many turns in advance as they are capable. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Definitely a game that heavily incentivizes planning ahead. In order to get the most from your actions, you have to really think about how the mancala-style action mechanic works. This can lead to performing actions in a seemingly strange order for the sole purpose of preparing for a single action. Great mental gymnastics and it's a great feeling when you can get some synergy going.

Another interesting aspect of the game is the time track, and how each player gets a say in how the game is paced. There can be certain situations where you can't do the action you want because it would trigger the end of a season and you're not quite ready for that. Conversely there may be times when you don't do the action you want and trigger a season end because you realize that other players are not prepared. This further adds to the things that need to be considered and planned for on your turn. Wonderful game if you want to practice thinking ahead.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I've had this on my wishlist for years and finally got to play it. Fantasic!
Okay, now clearly this is a game which will only appeal to a certain audience of gamers, but fortunately for me I'm in that category. It's a solid euro.

Mechanic - Card collection and personal action strategy. The mancala system really deepens the planning of your future turns.

Chance - There's a small bit with the card drawing and the peoples' demands but overall, it's pretty low.

Complexity - Not for casual gamers but once you're familiar with the basic actions you rarely need to consult the rules. Mastery though can take many plays.

Interaction - Moderate, but definitely there. I've heard some say the game is like solitare, but that's not true. Meeting the peoples' demands requires players to fight to grab resources before they're all taken up. Also, you can force the end of a "quarter year" (why they don't just call it a season is beyond me) by dumping a really long mancala cell and making the time counter spin past the end of the round. You also fight heavily for those senate bonuses. So yeah, there is definitely interaction.

Theme - I call it a "flat theme": the theme is strong but not dynamic. I've heard people say there's no theme and others say there is. I think the difference is just that the theme DOES fit the game well, it just doesn't change as the game progresses, so it might not "come alive" in that sense. For me, I do feel the ancient Roman atmosphere while playing, so there is a thematic atmosphere.

Components - Fine. It's nothing to rave about but it's not cheap.

Game Length - They say 30 mins/player and I think that's probably pretty close to accurate, depending on how deeply players spend calculating their moves on their turns. The first game does take some extra time to get the rules down but subsequent games should be much quicker.

Overall, one of my favorite games.

RATING: 7
Trajan is a tough game to rate, after my first play. I like games with strong central mechanisms, and like most Feld games, this is a collection of minigames with a million ways to score points. It's a puzzle game and a points optimizing game, something I usually dislike.

The game is held together by the Mancala mechanism, which really is 80% of the game. It makes choosing what to do very, very complex. And I'm not sure if I like that. Trajan isn't about choosing option A or B, since A obviously scores more points than B, but it's about gettting that damn mancala to work for you so you can actually perform option A when you want to. 

It's pretty gamey. It feels like complexity for complexity's sake. "Here you have some trivial minigames, now go through this tricky machine to do what you want to do." But that's not really being fair to the game. The Mancala puzzle is really interesting, it forces you to plan ahead and think about how the board will look after your actions. And the minigames do have just enough timing issues and interactivity and planning ahead to be more than just picking the one that gives the most points every time. 

You might want to build to take a tile now, before anyone else gets it, but you really want to go to the forum first, so you can get two extra build actions and take more tiles. But if you go to the forum, the stones in your mancala cups will change in such a way that you can't take the build action next turn. Are you ok with another turn setting it up? Those decisions are more interesting than any I've seen in other Feld games, as other factors than just optimizing points/action come into play.

So, I'm chalking the feeling of spending more time and energy figuring out the overly complex mancala than reacting to what goes on on the board and with the other players up to inexperience. I can see it becoming easier and more fluid with more plays, when I have a better idea of what is important in the order and spread of the colored stones and what I don't need to spend my mental energy on. I do have some nagging doubts that the game is too heavy for its own good, and that it doesn't have enough to offer other than the complex puzzle. I'll have to find out with more plays, but it's certainly interesting enough that I can see my rating going up a bit. I am, however, not really looking forward to playing it often; it's just too convoluted to get me enthusiastic. A 7, for now.

RATING: 5
This game is interesting and super frustrating to play. Getting the action pegs to line up to take the actions you want to take requires a lot of forethought. The extra actions mechanics make this even more frustrating when you see other players pull of moves that let them do a lot in the same turn. The different action regions aren't of equal value, so knowing that is important. It also has a 'feed your forces' mechanic each round to bring in some of that Agricola inspired frustration. Despite this all, I like the game. Somewhere between all that frustration there are a lot of little aspects to the game I enjoy. The thing that lets the negative aspects outshine the good aspects is how each area of the board has nothing to do with the others, making the game feel very empty.

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:nostar: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:nostar::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: 9
Perhaps the best game I've played this year. Reminds me quite a bit of Castles of Burgundy except the mechanics make a lot more sense to me here. Everything just flows and makes a 2 hour game seem like it takes half the time.

All the different actions seem very balanced. Chaining actions is key to victory and you'll know you didn't plan well if you perform only one action on a turn. Nearly everything rewards points, so make sure you build towards a powerful end game bonus.

Overall: Just all around excellent. Feld's greatest achievement in my opinion.

Edit: this one was surpassed by Amerigo for my favorite Feld design, but this still holds up over many plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
While I love many of Feld's games, I find this one to be one of his very best - which is high praise indeed. The mancala mechanic is obviously the main "gimmick" of this game, and on the first few plays it looks like it's been rather pasted on to a fairly standard Eurogame, or even an unrelated set of such games. But after more plays, one realises that this is not true - every action can affect every other, due to forum tiles picked up via the forum or military, or bonus tiles from the senate which could require you to do almost anything else in the game to get points from them. And the mancala - together with the Trajan tiles, which between them affect all of the other actions - is what ties this all together, requiring very careful planning so that you can take the actions you want to when you want (not to mention trying to control the timing of the game). It is a brilliant design that rewards repeated plays. The only thing that prevents me rating this higher than 8 is that it is really a brain burner (one of the most the most brain-burny game I own, and I like brain burners), and there are gamers I know who I would prefer not to play this against.

RATING: 7.4
(+) The mancala rondel is brilliant!
(+) Did I mention the mancala rondel is utterly fascinating?
(+) Have I allready stated the mancala rondel is strangely addictive?
(+) Mancala ... rondel... *drool*

(-) Some minor concern about starting player having statistically more actions than other players. In a close game this can be significant.

I hope Feld or another game designer will recycle the mechanism and combine it with a better game in the future. Still, it's fair to say those mini games interconnect elegantly as far as gameplay is concerned and together they make for an interesting game.

(-) game lacks a story and has a very low 'fun-factor': players play individually and the rondel keeps everybody rather quiet and occupied.

A collage of euro mechanics stringed together with an ingenious, mancala mechanic to select your actions. I enjoyed playing Mancala with my family on vacations growing up, and the tactics involved in Mancala meshes quite well with the rest of the game.

Trajan tends to be a bit of a solitaire game, but you will have plenty of opportunities to prioritize certain actions to block or cutoff your opponents throughout the game while benefiting yourself. Many people refer to Trajan, and many other of Feld's games, to be a 'Point Salad' but this isn't exactly a deterrent for me. Is it you for?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This game is really good. Yes, it's very dry and it's difficult to feel a senator when almost everything you do gets you points and nothing else, but the art is quite good and it's sufficient to feel the flavor a little more. The Mancala mechanic is a bit irritating sometimes, expecially if you "give up" about it and just get what you can with the simplest moves, but with a bit of experience it becomes easier to manage, which in turn makes any game better cause, simply but, Trajan tiles and additional actions, and on a minor side managing the pace of the game by moving (or not) the right amount of tokens, are absolutely essential to win.

What makes this game a little boring is the painstackingly long setup, with all those tiles you have to order, mix, turn over and then put in the right place, while order, mix and turn over the others you will have to put in later, and the ones you will have to remove. Since the tiles are not random at all, and it's vital for all of them to be used only once and then removed if not picked, it's very easy to mess things up. Also, it's a rare game the one where you don't have to check the rules over and over, cause they are very detailed and full of minutae, and not very intuitive after all, expecially the ones involving the cards. Also again, it's very important to NOT forget about moving the time counter after a move, which seems to happen very often and require some very boring backtracking. 

Apart from those rules/component issues, another problem is that this is a very runaway-leader game. Once a player gets some additional actions tiles and a dominant position in points (the senate is the prime offender) it's very difficult to the other players to keep up, which can lead to the last parts of the game being almost useless to play. However, this is a problem that is fixed with repeated games and by gaining experience.

An overrated problem is, instead, the supposed lack of players interaction. While players interaction it's surely not that primary point of this game, it's very present and it's incredibly easy and mean to deprive an opponent of a vital tile at the worse possible moment. Also, there are lot of occasions when multiple actions are available but some of them are the best only if you can do them before the others, and so you have to choose.

All in all, a very solid and good game that, maybe, could have been designed a little better to make even less intimidating than it looks.

RATING: 8
分數
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Played this late in the evening at UK Games Expo, with a fried brain and sat with the board upside-down to me. As I actually did very well, I thought this was a very satisfying and well-designed game. 

The mancala is interesting - it drives your play and there are ways it can be exploited, ways it can trip you up. It ties in to three elements of the game - how you choose what action to take, whether you get a bonus tile (if available)  for the action you take, and how much you push the game clock on. Similar sorts of choices, decisions and benefits to rondel systems. At the moment |I'm finding the 'push the game clock' element to be of most tactical significance - forcing the early end of a turn can deprive your opponent of points, whilst you make sure to score easy quick points early on. It has the potential to be baffling or induce AP, but I found it pretty intuitive to use.

Components-wise the game is a pleasing package. There is a bit of set up and tear down involved owing to the million and one pieces, manageable in about ten minutes each way. 

The game (in my playing experience) it feels closest to is Village - multiple ways to score, manipulation of time and game length. Trajan is less novel and somewhat less interactive, but it feels as though there may be more to explore in the range of tactical options.

RATING: 5
After 1 play I have a couple of major complaints. I'll probably give this another try as I am generally a big admirer of Feld's work but wow... for now these things really killed the game for me.

1. What the heck does any of this have to do with the Roman Empire? I mean seriously.

2. I found the mancala integration to be profoundly irritating. It needlessly layers complication and obfuscation on top of what I think should be the simplest of observations and actions. What actions are available to me? How do I take the action I wish to take? Sometimes it will be OK. Sometimes you'll want to drop a couple of colored tokens and you'll know where you want to put them or at least have some strong indicator of where they should land. But there will also be plenty of other times where you'll drop 5 or 6 colored tokens in 5 or 6 distinct places and there will literally be SEVERAL HUNDREDS of options ... and there will be virtually no way for you to weed out the good from bad options. It's worse than your typical non-decision. It's a non-decision of an incredible magnitude of options.

RATING: 7
Oh let me count the ways... there are to score victory points. How Stefan Feld balances and play tests all these multiple paths to victory games I have no idea. Somehow it seems to work though. Plus, I like the theme even if it is a bit thin.

So here you've got again a very limited number of actions you can perform on your turn. Like Castles of Burgundy you can have a particularly satisfying turn where you've managed a chain a Trajan tile action together with a double special action tile for a big turn. Other turns you'll find it's little baby steps as you plan for another big move a few turns away. All the while you're trying to time everything you want to fit in before the end of the quarter. Add a few races with your opponents here and there - mainly for shipping and provinces, but especially in the Senate. In the end you have a suitable brain burner that works equally well with 2-4 players.

Driving all this is the mancala, which is controls the pace of the game, the actions you can select and the Trajan tiles you can activate. It dispenses with the luck of the dice and puts you firmly in control and responsible for the good and the bad. I enjoyed a similar system in Finca, but here you've got your own rondel and there's nobody to interfere with yours... well, except that you have to keep an eye on where they can go with theirs before you.

It's well done and it's a good game I'm keen on exploring further. It might be my favourite new game from 2011.

RATING: 8

So, the players constantly have to decide if they want to do an action, or better to perform another one to win the bonus.

RATING: 7.9
I really like Trajan, and I feel very lucky that my SO does as well. In fact, she caught on so quickly that our plays are 3-1 for her so far.

Castle of Burgundy was a two-player grand slam for us, and Trajan seems to be only a small step up in complexity from Castles.

The mancala mechanism is really neat because, just as the main game tasks you with running the Roman empire efficiently (through trade, construction, political and military avenues), so too does this 'mini-game' challenge you to utilize your mancala stones with maximum efficiency. This game-within-a-game has a real puzzle-ly feel that truly sets Trajan apart in the field of strategy games.

The game still feels kind of tactical as opposed to strategic at times, and you may find yourself settling on short-term gains and reacting to your opponent(s) more often than setting up your big turns, but, boy, when those big turns do come through, they are delicious with a laurel-wreathed cherry on top!

I very heartily recommend this title to anyone who has enjoyed Castles of Burgundy and would like to try something equally abstract but also equally satisfying.

Oh, and if you haven't tried Castles of Burgundy yet, what are you waiting for??!!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A game where every action scores points, but some actions score more points than others, some have more risk with a higher potential payoff while others are guaranteed but with lower payoffs.  Some of the actions are inherently inferior to others most of the time, but in some situations become very valuable.  The winner is the player who most efficiently uses their turns to score points, strategically planning their future actions with occasional tactical decisions based on the current state of the board.

The entire game revolves around effectively positioning the action markers on your mancala trays to accomplish Trajan tiles and to take the actions you want when you want them.  Players who enjoy planning several moves ahead will love this game as the manacala mechanic is brilliant.

This is mostly an abstract game with a pasted on theme, so if you want to play a game about Rome look elsewhere.  Player interaction is fairly low, but those who ignore what other players are doing will probably not win.  Occasional situations in the miltary, construction, and senate areas can allow one player to have a large impact on other player's scores.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Trajan is the funfair of Euros; it's an assortment of minigames glued together by a mancala mechanism. It's easy to assume that the game's all about manipulating the mancala to get your desired actions, but you will also have to account for opponents muscling into your scoring paths and manipulating the speed of a round. The addition of self-imposed objectives (in the form of Trajan tiles) to up the difficulty of puzzle-solving is a very clever touch and one of my favourite aspects of the game.

After several games spread over a generous time span, I've come to appreciate the disjointed minigames. By minimising the ripple effect that an action in one particular area has on the rest of the game, decision-making is kept breezy. I also like how the relative scoring value of the minigames shift according to the number of players. (This is primarily meant to be played with either 3 or 4 players though.)

If anything, perhaps I would have liked a greater integration of theme and some originality in the minigames. And like most Feld designs, Trajan exists more as an exhibition piece for a single cool mechanism. But there is no denying it is good at what it does; it appeals to the puzzle-loving side of me with just the right amount of pokes and prods from the changing game state to keep me on my toes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9.25
#14 on my Top 100 Games of All Time (2016)
#16 on my Top 100 Games of All Time (2015)

My second favorite Feld! Perhaps just because of the amount I have played it. The mancala aspect of this game ensures that you will be doing some long-term planning. However, you are constantly assessing the board to adjust your plans as necessary. Like most Felds, there are multiple paths to victory as you can gain points from a variety of strategies. I believe that I often find myself going for the same angle in a lot of Euros, but somehow Trajan manages to always have me changing things up. Another thing I like about this game is that you don't have much downtime, even though it is turn-based, because each individual turn goes quickly, assuming you are not playing with anyone that suffers from analysis paralysis.

Acquired: February 2013 - CSI

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Weird mixture of city building in Rome, and Mancala. You have a rondel with 6 fields and 12 tokens. When it's your turn, you choose one of the fields, take all the tokens from there, distribute them onto the next (clockwise) fields, and can then do the action on the last field that got a token. Additionally, the tokens have different colours, and if you manage to produce certain combinations in one field, you get a bonus action. You can buy those bonus tokens (showing necessary colour combinations) yourself and try to place them in such a way that you will be able to complete the colour combination later. So, uhm, yeah. Let's call the action selection mechanism "interesting".

RATING: 8.75
Played my first game last night with two other people who played once before. Theme is good ( I believe that all boardgames are pretty much abstracts). Lots of stuff going on with some interaction. An example would be to grab a construction tile to keep someone from getting the 3rd or 4th tile because 3-of-a-kind equals 10 VP and 4-of-a-kind equals 20 VP. 
 I look at the "Mancala" part of the game as all the variables that are present in the building of an empire.
I'm sure this game is similar to many other games and my experience is limited in boardgames so I can't compare it to anything else out there. 
 If you want a Euro-style brain burner then this would be good pickup.

Edit: Played again Tuesday and won. Definitely a set collecting exercise but has many paths to victory. You can't try to do everything if you want to win but you have to be adaptable to the changing conditions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
I finally got to try this game, from way back (seemingly, although I guess it's only been 6 months) at Essen, at Gathering '12! I've really been enjoying Feld's recent games, like Macao, Luna, and Burgundy, as well as older games like Roma which I discovered just this past year. This is yet another very cool game! I don't think I like it quite as much as Macao, but it's pretty close!

It's an action-selection game which uses a Mancala-like system where the bowls are in a circle, and each of the 6 stops in the circle corresponds to a different action (acquire various tiles, place meeples into scoring areas, etc). In addition, the game starts with 3 Trajan tiles on 3 of these stops, each with their own action. It takes some planning to figure out how to set up your pieces such that you can take the actions you want in the order you desire. The speed at which people move through their Mancala circle sets the pace of the game -- I think it's a total of 24 steps around a timer circle, 4 times before the game ends (and there is, of course, a little bit of pain that happens after 3 of those trips if you haven't collected the right stuff).

Great game ... will probably look for a copy myself!
