=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After one play this may have become my favorite Feld game.

Most of the game is standard Feld fare.  Players take turns doing one of six different actions to acquire victory points.  Each action relates to a different mini-game style mechanic that scores you points in different ways.

The unique mechanic in this game is how the actions are actually selected.  Each player has a wheel with 6 trays and 12 different action tokens.  These tokens are moved around the wheel and where they end up is the action that player can take.  Each action token is also one of 6 different colors and tiles are assigned to different trays that need 2 action tokens to accomplish.  When those action tokens are in those trays the tile is accomplished giving the player points and a bonus action.

The game plays at a pretty decent clip, isn't terribly complicated, and I love both the variety of actions and paths players can take as well as the unique action wheel mechanic.

RATING: 6
While this is a fine game, I fear it suffers from the cult of the new, in that few are willing to talk about its weaknesses. The mechanics are very good, but the rules could be more clearly translated, and the theme is very much pasted on - this is, honestly, simply a well designed Euro, with elegant mechanics, but very little going for it beyond those mechanics, which, in the end, are simply another way of "shifting cubes." In other words, it may wear out its welcome quickly for you if you are looking for a game whose theme and environment particularly excitement. 

Not a bad game, by any means, and certainly deserves admiration and respect, but again, I don't know if it's really "Exciting" enough to have a great deal of sustainability/replayability in the long term.

RATING: 5
The game feels v much like Macao and Die burgen in that small incremental actions can be linked together in phases - also in that there a lot of points in the game and lot of ways  of getting theem. The Manacala/Rondel is frustratingly brilliant - after one play I am concerned that the back end Vp generators are not as interesting as how the actions string together (Vanuatu from the same year at Essen has raised the same concerns for me)  The theme is less of a paste on than most other recent Feld designs, however its still wafer thin. I tend to like Feld's post 2008 designs on first play and grow to dislike them after repeated plays - I hope that this will not follow that path.

Alas, a second game and its gone down in my estimation, second game felt as solitaire as the first with a little competition for the military spaces. otherwise, players focused on own rondel. Too many point scoring routes for competition to be very meaningful. Moreover, the linked actions feel like a math exercise. Admirable, but tedious

It is nicely produced and the rules are clear.

RATING: 8
A common eurogame design pattern these days is to unify several simple, well-worn subsystems through some mechanical novelty which justifies the game's existence -- it's not [i]just[/i] a knockoff of X, Y and Z because actions are resolved by balancing cards on your nose and no-one has ever done that in a game before! The example I always point to is Macao, which survives on the strength of its weird cube-rondel "windrose".

Well, Trajan is like Macao+, lessening the focus on damage mitigation while drastically increasing the level of forced planning. Replacing the windrose is a mancala rondel with twelve cylinders in six colours spread across six action spaces. Choosing an action redistributes the cylinders; having the correct cylinders on a given space can activate a "Trajan tile" assigned there, providing victory points and/or another bonus.

Most rondel and worker placement games feature a strong tension between efficiency of action and limited opportunities -- it's better to do action A before action B, but what if the round ends or another player gets to B first? Trajan cranks this up to eleven. Not only do you have to manage your actions on the rondel, you have to manage your cylinders for future actions while obtaining and activating Trajan tiles. The builder action can provide instant bonus actions; the forum and military actions can provide action-tiles which allow a given action to be executed twice instead of once (or three times if you also have the "X2" bonus-bonus from the relevant Trajan tile!). Planning for efficient actions is delicious AP hell, and of course your opponents are constantly stealing your opportunities and ruining eveything.

I do have some misgiving about the impact of luck, particularly the uncertain payoff from helmet/fire/food Trajan tiles and the commodity draw, but the mancala is so fantastic that it's still my favourite Feld game and possibly my game of the year for 2011. Brilliant.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
A játék gyakorlatilag absztrakt. Senki se várja, hogy az ókori Róma világába csöppen. Viszont nagyon jó. A szokott mechanizmus-mix Feldtől. Kiváló, önmagukban is izgalmas mechanizmusok jól összerakva és a rotán való haladás is nagyon izgalmas. Nagyon egyben van és bár rengeteg féle mechanizmus és ennek következtében rengeteg szabályrészlet van a játékban, mégis jól érthető, logikus átlátható. Remek, agyégető játék.
Sokat kell vele játszani, hogy az ember ne csak úgy érzésre döntsön, döntögessen, hanem tudatosan. Az ezernyi lehetőség és számolhatóság AP-sé teheti, de az is tény, hogya többiek köre alatt bőven van idő tervezgetni és számolgatni. Valószínűleg 5-10 játék után mutatja magát igazán a Trajan. A játékidő korrekt, kivételesen nincs semmi hazugság a 30 perc/játékos oké.

RATING: 9
Definitely my favorite game of 2012.  I would reserve the "point salad" moniker for Bora Bora over Trajan, since the individual scoring components seem to fit better to me than Bora. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The mancala mechanic is interesting, and forces you to plan out two, three, or even four turns in advance. This advanced planning adds interest, and gives you something to do while the other players are taking their turns. If not for the mancala mechanic, I would probably complain about downtime and/or the lack of serious interaction. You can steal certain chits that other players may want. You can hurry the game along in order to screw others out of turns (this is actually a solid mechanic, and I should probably give it more credit). However, Trajan still feels solitaire-ish to me.

Trajan is fiddly, and most certainly all about maximizing a lot of little points. As in many of these types of games, there are a number of different point "stations" that you hit up for chits that translate into VPs. (Does anyone else feel like games with "station" end up feeling like a mish-mash of little mini-games? See also Tribune.) It's a fun game. I enjoyed it. But it's not elegant. And it's not really innovative beyond the mancala mechanic. What Trajan really makes me want to do is try out some more mancala games!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The mancala mechanism is just plain cool.  It lives up to the billing and makes for a unique centerpiece to the game.  The actions available to a player consist of a Mac Gerdts type Rondel where the number of spaces you can go are based upon the number of pieces in a dish.  It is the centerpiece and focal point, and truly introduces a neat little game to figure out and maximize.  There is not interaction in this portion of the game, and while it will be a turn off to some, I enjoy it as it allows some think ahead.  (This game is brutal with over-analyzers).  

Everything else is about timing.  There is a race aspect for tile collection for three different sub games, a sub game with set collection, and a "Most votes" sub game.  The balancing between the sub contests and the timing between make the mancala rondel a unique experience.  The winner of the most votes gets a reward altering their valuation.  This prevents the game from being scripted and stale, and also doesn't allow for a "best" strategy.

There is also a way to match colors and tiles at a dish location to gain a bonus action. 

I'm only two plays in, but this game is so clever.  I enjoy the decisions I'm faced with, and I look forward to increasing my skill.  Our second 2 player game took 1.5 hours including set up and take down.  Look out.

I can't imagine playing this with an AP prone player.  I haven't, and I will refuse to.

After further reflection, the sub games are just well disguised (and flexible) tech trees.  

Just awesome.  10 potential.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Usually I don't hold off on commenting on a game. This one I did. I had to really take my time to digest this one. Yeah this game is awesome.  

I honestly feel this one plays every bit as well at 2 as 3 or even 4 players. Such a perfect game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Awesome gamer's game. Mancala core system 'makes' the game, it is ellegant and injects a good dose of depth to the game. Trajan deserves nearly everything I want in a good euro for 60-90 min:

-Long term thinking (strategy): Nearly from the beginning of the game you have to think about the endgame scoring: bonus tiles you are earning during the game, leads you to focus in something to get many endgame points (shipping, construction, military, senate, demands)

-Medium term thinking: Mancala 'look ahead', how to accomplish demand neeeds for that quarter.

-Tactical decisions: you have often to react to your opponents: go first to the forum when a new quarter starts, pick any important tile when situation is able to, senate race...

-A bit of spatial reasoning: Construction and military actions deserves a bit of 'area control' thinking

-Enough randomness to make the game different every time but not to hurt the players.

-60-90 min playtime: This game has a lot of going on for such playtime.

-Stressful: Acomplishing citizen demands adds some stress to the game. There is also the feel you haven´t time to do everything you want.

Nearly everything you do in Trajan scores, the key is to be as efficient as you can in your actions. Trajan deserves a nice balance between planning/mancala efficiency, and adapting your play to which is happening on the board.

Luck plays a small role here (commodity cards, forum and bonus tiles...), but clever play is a lot more important and the best player will win nearly every day.

Trajan scales wonderfully from 2 to 4 players. A bit more strategical and less competitive with 2, and more tactical and competitive with 4 but I enjoy it with any number.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
If you can get someone to play a second time, I think they'll have a hard time not liking Trajan. What other people call "points-salad" amounts to a heavily obfuscated goal structure. It's hard to tell people what to do to win the game. You need the most points, but you get points for everything. Seeing through the hail-storm of points is part of the puzzle, and that requires at least a second play. The manacala-board action selection mechanism will take longer than that to get the hang of, but I feel like its addictive as soon as you have limited success with it. If it's hanging you up every turn in your first game, the game will feel arduous. But if you can string together a 2 or 3 turn combo that you're proud off, you'll get the bug. I find the whole experience very satisfying, and, 9 plays in, I'm really enjoying the cat-and-mouse game created by giving players control of the length of a year. Trying to time thing so that you have first play at the beginning of a year is really important. Because of this, I disagree with those who say this game is equally good at every player count. I like 2 and 3 the best, because you get a little more control over this mechanic. All in all, a rich and rewarding game that I'll be playing for years.

RATING: 10
Might be even better than Castles of Burgundy. Great fun, comes out close almost every time, even fun when I lose. Great mix of strategy and tactics, the "luck" is somewhat in the tiles that come out, but more in the chaos you develop in your own mancala. So it's self-induced "luck," which is great. 

We've played it 2p and 3p, and it's great at both of those counts. Haven't tried 4. For now, we prefer a house rule--we remove the wild-card tiles for the construction area. Those tiles sway the game too much, we think, though we may reconsider at some point.

RATING: 7.5
Another interesting game from Stefan Feld, who is rapidly becoming one of my favourite designers! As always with mr Feld's games, this game is all about damage control and a dwindling number of options, resulting in agonizing decisions and quite a bit of "turn angst". This time, the fresh mechanism is a Mancala rondel - an interesting idea that plays out like a personal puzzle within the main game, unfortunately leading to a decrease in interactivity with other players. Even though the artwork of the game does not appeal to me, the components of the game are of an excellent quality - certainly much better than the quality of most Alea games. All in all, I would love to play this game again, but I do not need to own it. But that's only because I slightly prefer Stefan's more accessible games (like Notre Dame and In The Year Of The Dragon). The explanation of this game is pretty daunting.

RATING: 5
One of the players I play with described this as six separate minigames, all governed by a larger game of [gameid=2448].  It's a glib, but apt, criticism. The minigames don't fit well together, and they are almost completely separated.  The little interaction that does occur is subsumed in the Mancala minigame on individual player boards.  There's no theme here of any significance, and the gameplay is dry as dust.  It's one of the best examples of JASE - Just Another Soulless Euro.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Yet another brilliant Feld game. It's amazing the roll he's on with Macao, Burgund, and Trajan. Even the lesser lights like Speicherstadt are good games.

Fast turns with low downtime, as your mancala mechanism generally means you can only choose from 2 or 3 actions. But, with so many ways to use those actions on the main board, the decision space is deliciously large. Great fun to set yourself up from turn to turn (I'll move this here here and here this turn, then next turn I'll move that and that, setting up blue and yellow together, so the turn after that I'll do this which will give me this tile for 9 points...)

The special thing about this game is the "wheel". It has 6 bowls in wich you place meeples. Each bowl represents an action on the board. If you want to act one out you have to move the meeples so that the last meeples stops in the bowl you want to act out. 

Nice mechanic, wich can get you into trouble sometimes.

RATING: 2
UPDATE: Traded. Not as good as Castles of Burgundy. Just a lever-pulling VP generating optimisation game and transparently so. No feeling of building anything or doing anything interesting at all.

UPDATE: With repeated plays it's very very dry. The mancala feels like a bit of a gimmick designed to stop you taking the actions you want.

Deep game that we're exploring in the 100 Play Challenge. It's better than Castles of Burgandy and Macao, but less fun than Luna - in my opinion. It feels pretty dry and abstract. The mancala mechanic is extremely interesting and adds lots of strategic depth - worth trying or buying for that alone.

The component quality is as high as Luna, and significantly better than Castles and Macao from the Alea line.

The game rewards repeated play - I didn't understand how to work the mancala and take the actions I wanted until my third play or so.

RATING: 7
I'm not generally a fan of Feld's self-absorbed point salad games, but the mancala-esque action mechanism in Trajan really tickles my fancy. Plus, this isn't really a point salad, as there is a focus on mid and long range goals that are constantly under threat by your opponents. There's just enough interaction here to keep things tense, and the random tile draw keeps the puzzle fresh. As much as I respect In the Year of the Dragon, I just have more fun playing this; I think this might be my favorite Feld.

Spiel 2012
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
What an amazing idea to use the main Mancala mechanism as the main engine for a complex Euro. Feld absolutely knocked it out of the park with this one. The game is essentially 5 or 6 mini-games, with each players Mancala board determining exactly which one they get to play on any given turn. The only randomness to that is the starting arrangement of your Mancala. After that, you are in complete control. The better you plan, the better you will be able to fire off whatever move on whatever mini-game you want. Of course, being a Feld game, if you plan well you can even get multiple moves on a single turn, if you can combo out. There is so much to think about and so many ways to make clever moves that you rarely if ever, have a turn that can run on autopilot. This is Feld's best, by far. Buy it.

RATING: 7
Like the game, but, it really felt like I was playing around with a string of mechanisms that could get me to the end of the game in a variety of ways, only to joylessly win points. I know that is the 'Feld' expectation, but for some reason 'Trajan' was really stripped of no other motivator than to acquire points, and if not for the Rondel, which I am partial to in any game, I would find no real fun in this game. Like playing on a calculator I guess. 

I really enjoy 'Macau' and 'In the Year of the Dragon', but I felt that those instituted mechanisms to move the games along, with some semblance of thematic sense in the action, where as in Trajan, I felt the mechanisms were just there for no particular reason than to have another choice in the matter.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A Eurogame that Eurogamers will probably love and others might enjoy too.  The much talked about Rondel action mechanic is as interesting to manipulate to your advantage as you've heard and the way the different parts of the board intereact lead to more player blocking/interaction than you might expect from what looks like a solitaire exxercise at first glance.  I was impressed how non-fiddly it is despite the large number of small bits too.

The theme is paper thin... but at least all the actions make sense in one way or another.  Certainly a game I'd recommend to folks looking for a middleweight Euro.

RATING: 9
Szuper játék! :cool: Ritkán szoktam egy játék után véleményt írni, de ez különösen tetszett. Egyrészt nincs feleslegesen túlbonyolítva, ugyanis 6 alapakcióra épül a játék. Tetszik az h lehet manipulálni a játék idejét (az időjelzővel). Sokat lehet taktikázni a játékoskör első fázisánál, ahol akciójelzőinket variálgatjuk.  

Ami különösen tetszik benne az hogy lehet hatalmasakat durrantani benne, szinte szárnyra kelnek pontozókorongjaink :) Már az első partyban volt egy 7 akciós köröm (Trajan lapka teljestése, céltárca akció (építkezés), 2x építkezés combó és az építési területen szerezett építéslapkák segítségével +3 akció, ugyanis még egyik fajtából sem volt a játékostáblámon.) Ez hatalmas játékélményt nyújtott!!! Amikor durrant valamelyik játékos az fokozza a feszültséget a játékostársaiban, ugyanis jócskán le tudnak maradni. :D

Kevés konfrontáció, viszonylag pörgős játékmenet, sok taktikai és stratégiai manőverrel lehet nyerni. Kiválóan felüdítő játék. Megérdemli részemről a magas pontozást, benne van a TOP 10-be.

RATING: 8
After 2+ Plays: Feld's games are starting to feel a little too familiar.  This game's structure is just so similar to Macao and Castles of Burgundy - one interesting mechanic with a bunch of little ways to score points.  I enjoy those games' central mechanics more, so I won't be suggesting this one. 

After 5+ plays: I've come around a bit on this game.  I do still think these games are too similar, but I continue to enjoy them all, so I'm bumping this from a 7 to an 8.

RATING: 7.5
I like the action selection mechanic which reminds me to an extent of Finca, albeit its based on Mencala. There is a lot going on in this game , which is great, and there are multiple paths to get stacks of victory points. You are not building an 'engine' so you can change direction. The theme is very tacked on , but it is enjoyable to play. My only concern is that its a bit too long for my liking - I would like to play it with 3 rather than 4 to see if its the same game , but shorter.
